ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
Glossary of Technical Terms
AGGREGATE: Uncrushed or crushed gravel, stone or rock or artifically pro-
duced inorganic materials which form the major part of concrete.
In Gecamines, aggregate is produced by crushing limestone rock.
CLINKER:    The agglomerated or partly fused residue, following heating in
air to convert sulphide concentrates to oxides.
CONCENTRATOR: A plant which separates ore into economically valuable pro-
ducts (concentrates) and rejects (tailings). Usually refers to
a plant which utilizes the physical properties of the ore-mag-
netism, specific gravity.
DOLOIITE:  A mineral.  A carbonate of magnesium and calcium.  Also applies
to those rocks that approximate the mineral dolomite in composi-
tion.
ELECTROSTATIC PRECIPITATION: Air or gases are drawn through a high potential
direct current field causing the dust particles to acquire an,
electrical charge, and deposit themselves on the collecting sur-
face (the anode). One of the more efficient dust collection
methods.
ELECTRO-WINNING: Recovery of a metal by means of electrochemical processes.
In GECAMINES, the copper in an acidified copper sulphate solu-
tion is deposited on the cathode of an electrolytic bath. The
product, CATHODE COPPER, requires melting in a furnace to be
marketable as electrolytic copper.
FILTRATION: A process for separating solids from liquids by allowing the
liquids to pass through a finely woven cloth or gauze which
retains the solids. A vacuum or pressure is used to accelerate
the process.
FLOTATION:  A method of separation in which a froth created in water is
used, with a variety of reagents, to float finely ground minerals
whereas other minerals sink.
GERMANIUM: A grayish-white rare metallic element.  Its main use, having
exceptional properties as a semi-conductor, is in the manufacture
of solid rectifiers or diodes, and in transistors.
HYDRO-METALLURGY: The treatment of ores or concentrates by dissolution of
some component and its subsequent recovery from the solution.



ANNEX 1
Page 2
LEACHING:   The extraction of a soluble metallic compound from an ore or
concentrate by selectively dissolving in a suitable solvent,
such as water or sulphuric acid. The solvent is usually recovered
by precipitation of the metal or metals.
LIGNITE:    A coal of low rank with a high inherent moisture and volatile
matter.
ORE:        A naturally occurring mineral, or minerals, which can be mined at
a time, at a place, and at a benefit.
PELLETIZING: A method in which finely divided material is rolled in a drum
or on an inclined disk, so that the particles cling together in
small spherical pellets.  Usually requires the addition of heat
or reagents to ensure formation of pellets.
PRECAMBRIAN: Oldest rocks on the geological scale. Those rocks formed
before evidence of life first appeared on earth.
REFINING:   The purification of crude metallic products.  In copper, repre-
sents the final stage in the processing to relatively pure netal.
Usually accomplished by electro or fire methodls. Product is
usually called ELECTROLYTIC COPPER.
REVERBERATORY FUTRNACE: A furnace, with a shallow hearth, having a roof that
deflects the flame and radiates heat on the surface of the
charge.
ROASTING:   Heating an ore or concentrate to effect some chemical change
that will facilitate smelting. For sulphide ores, the roasting
converts the sulphides to oxides.
SEDIMENTARY: Rocks formed by the accumulation of sediments in water, or
from air.
SELECTIVE PRECIPITATION: The precipitation of specific metals in a solution
by changing its acidity, or through addition of reagents or heat.
SMELTING:   A metallurgical operation in which metal is separated by fusion
from those impurities with which it may be chemically combined
or physically mixed.  In copper, the metal is usually obtained
as a molten matte. The product is usually 70% copper.
STRATIFORM: A synonym for sedimentary rocks, which are usually bedded or
layered.



NTNEX 1
Page 3
SUPERGENETIC:  Solution of metals by surface w.zaters from an ore deposit and
its redeposition elsewhere.
THICKENING: The process of concentrating solids in suspension by settling
to obtain a thicker pulp and rejectin! some of the liquid.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
GECAMINES - HISTORY
1798      Earliest recorded mention of copper workings in Shaba (Katanga).
1890 -    First Belgian expedition to Shaba and subsequent surveying of
1905    the main mineral deposits in the area by the "Comite Speciale du
Katanga" (CSK) (founded in 1900) and "Tanganyika Concessions
Limited" (TCL) (founded 1899). The main objective was the
extraction of gold, but the considerable copper deposits soon
became apparent. The resulting discoveries were to be operated
in common, the necessary capital being supplied by the two parties
and the profits to be distributed at the rate of 60% for the
Comite Special du Katanga and 40% for the Tanganyika Concessions
Limited.
1906      Foundation of Union Miniere du Haut-Katanga (UMHK).  Comite
Special du Katanga in agreement with Tanganyika Concessions
Limited brought to Union Miniere the results of the mining
studies and prospections carried out in,Katanga, as well as
the rights stipulated in an agreement outlining the geo-
graphical limits of Union Miniere's activities and setting
forth the operations to be undertaken by the same. The
initial capital was set at 10 million Belgian francs, repre-
sented by 100,000 shares, subscribed 50-50 by the Societe
Generale de Belgique and TCL. A further 100,000 dividend
shares were assigned to CSK in consideration of its contri-
bution to be shared with TCL in the ratio of 60:40.
1907      Preliminary prospecting work terminated, continuing problems
with transport, endemic diseases and labor recruiting. Cold
first mined from the Ruwe mine; the working of the gold mines
as such was discontinued in 1911.
1910      The Rhodesian railway reached Lubumbashi (Elisabethville)
providing a link with South Africa and later on with Beira
(Mozambique). For transport in the Shaba province, steam
traction engines were used.
1911      Start-up of crude copper smelting at the Lubumbashi smelting
works.  The minerals are mined at the Etoile copper mine
near Lubumbashi.  Copper production cost amount to about
double the sales price.  Union Miniere's financial diffi-
culties were overcome by a 2-1/2 million BF capital increase
and a 10 million BF bond issue.



ANNEX 2-1
Page 2
1913      Production rose to 7,400 tons of copper.  Start-up of a
second furnace at Lubumbashi and the Kambove and Luisha
mines. Steam-powered mechanical shovels were being intro-
duced in the mines. Railway line reached Kambove, thus
providing transport within Katanga. Labor was recruited in
Mozambique and in Nyasaland to compensate for the lack of
local manpower.
1918      Mining of cassiterite (tin oxide) begins.  The ore was
worked until 1948. 85,000 tons of copper produced.
1919      The sale of Union Miniere products were entrusted to the
Societe Generale des Minerais (S.G.M.).
1921      First gold and silver recovered by refining blister
copper at the Societe Generale Metallurgique de
Hoboken, Belgium. Start-up of the Panda concentrator.
1922      Copper production reached 43,000 tons per year.  Uranium
bearing ore was worked to produce radium. This production
was stopped in 1960. Preparatory work for the exploitation
of the Kipushi mine; Union Miniere created two subsidiary
companies -- the construction and real estate firm "Compagnie
Fonciere du Katanga" (COFOKA) and the coal mining enterprise
"Charbonnage de Luena."
1924      First cobalt-copper alloy cast.
1927      Foundation of the "Minoteries du Katanga," another Union
Miniere subsidiary. The Panda plants were put into
operation with ore flotation installation, reverberatory
furnaces for the reduction of concentrates, electric
furnaces for the production of crude cobalt, thermal power
plant and laboratories.
1928      Railway reached Ilebo (Port-Francqui) opening up the national
rail-river route from Lubumbashi to Matadi. Union Miniere
experiments with a labor stabilization policy which led to
the settlement of the workers and their families close to
their work.
1929      Start-up of production of electrolytic copper at the
Likasi-Shituru plant. Production reached 137,000 tons
of copper, 700 tons of cobalt and 550 tons of tin ore.
Foundation of UMRK subsidiary "SOGECHIM" -- "Societe Generale
Industrielle et Chemique du Katanga" for the production of
sulphuric acid and palm oil.



ANNEX 2-1
Page 3
1930      Commissioning of the first hydro-electric power station
on the Lufira River by "SOGEFOR" -- Societe Generale des
Forces Hydro-Electriques du Katanga. Union Mliniere formed
another power company -- Societe Generale Africaine
d'Electricite "SOGELEC."
1931      Railway link to Dilolo completed, thus opening up the
way to Lobito via Benguela Railways of Angola. As
the result of the economic and monetary crisis the price
of copper fell from about 14 cents per pound to about
4 cents. Union Minieres production dropped from 139,000
tons in 1930 to 54,000 tons in 1932.
1932      Foundation of "SUD-KAT" -- Societe de Recherches Minieres du
Sud-Katanga.
1937      Start-up of production of zinc concentrates.  By roasting
the latter, sulphuric acid was produced of which the leaching
plant at Shituru consumes large amounts. Increase of Union
Miniere's capital to 300 million BF and consolidation of the
different securities outstanding to 1,242,000 bearer shares.
1943      Production of uranium concentrates from ores previously
processed only for the recovery of radium. During 1940-44
800,000 tons of copper were shipped.
1941/42   Closing down of the Kambove and Luishia mines.  Development
and expansion of the Kolwezi, Musonoi, Kamoto and Ruwe mines
as well as the Kolwezi and Kipushi concentrators.
1947      Start-up of cobalt metal production at the Likasi-Shituru
plant.
1948      Societe Generale Metallurgique du Katanga (METALKAT) was created
in 1948 for the purpose of transforming zinc concentrates
into electrolytic metal.
1952      Electrification of the railways.  Three new hydro-electric power
plants were installed during the 1950-56 period corresponding to
an average yearly capacity of 2,500 million kwh.
1953      First recovery of germanium and cadmium from Kipushi ores.
1956      Production reached 247,000 tons of copper, 9,000 tons of cobalt,
203,000 tons of zinc concentrates.
1960      Commissioning of the semi-automatic copper and cobalt electrolysis
plants at Luilu (Kolwezi). Production reached 300,000 tons of copper.



ANNEX 2-1
Page 4
1962      Start-up of automatic operations at the Kambove and Kakanda con-
centrators. Labor force reached about 20,000 people.
1961-
1963   Short production interruption and subsequent rehabilitation
of facilities during the political and military events.
Deliveries of metals were not subject to interruption: however,
production dropped to a low of 269,000 tons of copper and 7,300
tons of cobalt in 1963.
1965      A convention between the Democratic Republic of the Congo and
the Katanga Company officially dissolved the Comite Special
du Katanga. 222,950 shares, 66,062 debentures and a nominal
certificate with 165,000 voting rights were transferred to the
Congolese Government, thus obtaining an 18% shareholding in
Union Miniere.
1967      The Congolese Government expropriated Union Miniere's assets
and confers them upon a new State-company -- GECOMINES (la
Generale Congolaise des Mines) constituted under Congolese
law with headquarters in Lubumbashi. On January 15, 1967
SGM and GECOMINES concluded a technical cooperation agreement
which assures the continuing operation of the production
facilities, the commercialization of GECOMINES' products and
a system of export financing.              1
1969      Amendments to the January 1967 convention regulated the com-
pensation question between Zaire and Union Miniere. The
compensation and technical cooperation fee was fixed at 6%
of gross sales for 15 years.
1970-
1974   GECAMINES (la Generale des (carrieres et des Mines du Zaire) 1/
initiated its first 5-year program (1970-74) which was to
increase copper production capacity from 360,000 tons per
year to 460,000 tons per year and cobalt production capacity
from 12,000 to 16,000 tons per year. New installations and
expansions included (i) exploitation of the Kamoto underground
mine, (ii) capacity expansions of the concentrators in Kalwezi,
Kambove and Kamoto, of the washing plant at Ruwe and the Luilu plants,
(iii) construction of an oxygen plant in Lubumbashi and (iv)
development of a new shaft at the Kipushi mine. Program
implementation proceeded according to schedule.
1/   GEOCOMINES was renamed GECAMINES in 1971.



ANNEX 2-1
Page 5
Renegotiation of the 1967 and 1969 conventions between tie Government of
Zaire, GECAMINES and SGMI. The new agreements reached in April 1974
stipulate:
(1)  as final compensation for Union Miniere's assets
Zaire will make a lump sum payment of 4 billion FB
(2)  a Zairian owned marketing company for the commerciali-
zation of mineral products will be created with the
assistance of SGM
(3) the creation of a copper refining and casting plant
in Bas-Zaire in cooperation with SGM
(4) technical assistance between SGM and GECAMINES
according to the latter demand
(5) Zaire guarantees for a limited time period the raw
material supply for Societe Metallurgique de lloboken.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSICN PROJECT
SUBSIDIARIES
1.        At the time of nationalization in 1967, GECAMINES had participations
in 11 subsidiaries in Zaire. The 1967 and 1969 convention between the Govern-
ment of Zaire and SGM, which regulated the compensation of Union Miniere's
assets, did not clearly stipulate the future of UMHK subsidiaries. Following
several disputes between the parties concerned, President Mobuto announced
on November 30, 1974 the nationalization of all former UMHK subsidiaries. The
concerned companies are being incorporated into existing State entities, such
as SNEL, GECAMINES, Office d'Alimentation, SONAS, etc. GECAMINES will own
and manage six of its ex-subsidiaries METALKAT, SOGECHIM, LA FONCIERE CML
(Ciments Metallurgique de Likasi), a flour mill (MINOTERIES DU KATANGA), the
coal mine Luena as well as several farms. All subsidiaries are directly re-
lated to GECAMINES metal production by producing intermediate products and
raw materials or processing GECAMINES' output.
A.   Coal Mine Luena
2.        GECAMINES has operated the Luena Coal Mine since 1967 when the
original owner "Societe de Charbonnages de la Luena" stopped production. On
August 16, 1973, the concessions of the "Societe de Charbonnages de la Luena"
were transferred to GECAMINES.
3.        The Luena Colliery has been exploiting the Kaluku deposit located
about 6 km south of Luena since 1962. At an annual production rate of 100,000
tons, the deposit will be depleted by 1976. The main consumers of the Luena
production have been GECAMINES (4,000-5,000 tons/month), CINSHABA (4,000-
5,000 tons/month), BRASSERIES SIMBA, VAP/LUBUMBASHI and AMATO-FRERES (300 tons/
month each).
4.        The Kaluku mine exploits a lignite coal with a high sulfur content
which is difficult to store or transport over long distances. The coal is
sorted and washed to yield 10-30 and 30-80 sized products.
5.        GECAMINES plans to increase its coal production in order to operate
a coal gasification unit (ANNEX 4-1, para. 45-49). After the depletion of the
Kaluku mine, the Company will reopen with modern mining techniques the Luena
and Kiaula mines which have been closed in 1960. The GECAMINES expansion
project includes 9 0.6 million for modernization of the Luena coal mine.
B.   UCS - Usines Chimigues de Shituru
6.        UCS (former SOGECHIM) was created in 1929.  Its installation includes
a sulfuric acid plant, a plant for palm oil hydrolysis and a water purifica-
tion plant.



ANNEX 2-2
Page 2
7.        The sulfuric acid plant is supplied by zinc concentrates from
Kipushi. Oxydation of the concentrate produces (i) zinc oxide which is trans-
ported and further processed in Kolwezi at UZK and (ii) sulfur anhydride
which after purification washing and drying gives sulfuric acid. Plant capac-
ity is 400 tons of sulfuric acid per day, which is used by the metallurgical
plants of Shituru and Luilu.
8.        GECAMINES requires refined palm oil as a reagent in the concentration
of its silicious oxide ores. USC operates a plant for palm oil hydrolysis in
Shituru which produces annually 5,000 tons of fat acid and 300 tons of glycer-
ine.
9.        The water purification plant treats water from the Panda River.
Daily consumption amounts to about 16,000 M3, of which GECAMINES retains one-
third for industrial uses and REGIDESO, the national water supply company,
distributes the remaining amount throughout Likasi.
10.       The financial position of ex-SOGECHIM has been excellent.  In 1973,
the sales volume reached 9 3.5 million with a net Income of X 0.7 million.
The subsidiary has no long-term debt, but equity of 9 3.1 million and a 2.0
current ratio. No notable expansions and capital investments have been en-
visaged during the seventies.
C.   SZARL - La Fonciere de Zaire
11.       La Fonciere, a real estate company, has been responsible for the
construction and maintenance of GECAMINES staff houses. After November 30,
1973, the management of non-GECAMINES property has been transferred to SONAS
(Societe Nationale d'Assurance). La Fonciere owns land and about 250 build-
ings in Shaba with an estimated value of 9 8.9 million. In 1973, the Company
realized a net profit of 9 1.4 million.
D.   CML-Cim       etalluriuesAejLkasi
12.       CML is the smallest of GECAMINES' subsidiaries.  Net fixed assets
in 1973 amounted to 9 173,000 and sales to 9 123,000. The Company produced
5,835 tons of filler, 2,248 tons of fibro-cement, 1,522 tons of ordinary ce-
ment and 32 tons of plaster. GECAMINES consumed about 65% of CNL's output.
E.   UZK - Usines de Zinc de Kolwezi
13.       UZK (formerly METALKAT) is located in Kolwezi and will be GECAMINES'
most important subsidiary. The company processes the zinc concentrate pro-
duced from the Kipushi ore. Recovery of the zinc ore involves the following
steps:
(a) Differential flotation of the ore in Kipushi yields a 26% copper
concentrate and 55% zinc concentrate.



ANNEX 2-2
Page 3
(b) Roasting of the zinc concentrate in the installation of UCS, in
Likasi (para. 6) recovers sulfuric acid and roasted zinc concentrate
with a content of 64% zinc, 1% copper, 0.3% cadmium.
(c)  At the UZK plants in Kolwezi the roasted concentrate is further
treated as described in Annex 2-4, para. 26 - 29, and yields
99.995% zinc ingots.
14.       The production facilities in Kolwezi employ about 670 people.  The
production capacity of electrolytic zinc plant is 72,000 tons per year, and
a small flotation plant established in 1970 allows the extraction of about
6,000 tons per year of concentrate from the leached residuals. In addition,
UZK recovers about 300 tons per year of cadmium.
15.       UZK (METALKAT), at the time of its incorporation in GECAMINES, had
a sound financial structure as indicated below:
UZK - Financial Indicators
(9 Million)
1972      1973
Fixed Assets                                   .      2.3        1.8
Current Assets                                        8.2       13.5
Equity                                                5.5        6.2
Current Liabilities                                   3.2        4.9
Long-term debt                                         -
Sales                                                18.2       22.6
Net Income                                            0.8        4.1
16.       The known Kipushi ore reserves can supply UZK for another 10 years
at a production rate of 72,000 tons of zinc content per year.  Geological
surveys are underway in Kipushi to ascertain additional zinc/copper ore re-
serves. If these studies prove sufficient reserves, UZK intends to modernize
its production facilities to allow a 97% metal recovery as compared to 86%
at present. Preliminary investment costs for the modernization have been
estimated at s 6 million; however, the project will only be realized in the
early 1980's.
Industrial Projects Department
July 1974

ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Legal Position
1.        La Generale des Carrieres et des Mines du Zaire (GECAMINES) --
created on January 2, 1967 -- is a state-ownedd company with headquarters in
Lubumbashi. The Company's statutes are defined as of September 14, 1972 and
amended by Law No. 74-011 dated July 10, 1974.
2.        The objectives of GECAMINES as defined by the statutes are (i)
exploration and exploitation of mineral deposits, (ii) the treatment of
minerals mined from these deposits, (iii) the sales of these mineral products
as well as (iv) all operations which are directly or indirectly related to
these activities.
3.        GECAMINES was managed originally by a Bdard of Directors comprising
a President (Mr. Kandolo Lopepe), five Directors, the General Manager and
the Deputy General Manager. On August 10, 1972 the Board of Directors was
dissolved as a result of Decree-law No. 72-034 and the Directors' functions
consequently ceased. Under GECAMINES' new statutes, published by Decree-law
No. 72-050, dated September 14, 1972, GECAMINES is under the supervision of
the President of the Republic and is managed by a General Manager assisted by
a Deputy General Manager, with full powers to conduct the operations of
the Company.
4.        Under Decree No. 72-311, dated August 10, 1972, Mr. Kandolo
Lopepe was appointed President of the Institut de Gestion du Portefeuille
- IGP (Portfolio Management Board) and given responsibility of permanent
liaison between GECAMINES and the Presidency of the Republic of Zaire.
GECAMINES' General Manager and his Deputy are appointed directly by the
President. Further, the Presidency of the Republic of Zaire has to authorize
(i) any debt in excess of one year and (ii) anv acquisition, liquidation of
transfer of financial assets.
5.        GECAMINES' financial operations have to be conducted according to
sound accounting and financial practices. The statutes require an annual
allocation of 5% of net profits to statutary reserves until these reserves
have reached 10% of the Company's equity capital.
B.   Organizational Structure
6.        Chart No. 1 and Chart No. 2 of this Annex give an overall view of
GECAMINES' organizational structure. GECAMINES is sub-divided into functional
departments such as Production, Finance, Purchasing, Marketing, etc., and
regional departments for the Western Group and the Central Group.  However,



ANNEX 2-3
Page 2
the regional directors have mainly administrative and representation functions
and do not direct the Company's production activities.
7.        GECAMINES' organizational structure reflects the Company's history.
Traditionally, all operational aspects were directed from Lubumbashi, but
financial, marketing and purchasing functions centered in Brussels. Conse-
quently, after nationalization GECAMINES had not only to take over UMHK
activities in Shaba, but had to define and form a new organizational entity
in Lubumbashi. This restructuration included:
(i) the creation of a Finance Department in 1971 and
introduction of a cost accounting system.
(ii) the strengthening of the Lubumbashi Purchasing
Department. At present about 35% of orders for
equipment, materials and supplies are channeled
through SCM in Brussels.
(iii) the creation of a Marketing Department in Lubumbashi.
It functions primarily as liaison and iniformation
office, since the commercialization of GECAMINES'
products were entrusted to SGM and in the future will
be handled by the Zairian marketing organization, SOZACOM
(Annex 3-4).
8.        The geographical distribution of GECAMINES' operations in three
centers (Western, Central and Southern Group) contrasts with the Company's
organizational structure which is geared towards Lubumbashi: For example,
the Production Department is grouped according to functions such as mines,
concentrators, metallurgical plants, instead of regional production centers.
An exception is the Western Group where a mining manager is responsible for
all mining activities in the Kolwezi area. Such a coordinating function is
urgently needed, since the Western Group accounts for about 65% of GECAMINES'
metal production. During the next 2 - 3 years, GECAMINES should review its
organizational structure and reassess the functional relationships vis-a-vis
an allocation of operational responsibilities.
C.   Management
9.        On November 30, 1973, Mr. Umba Kyamitala, a mining engineer with
more than ten years experience in GECAMINES, replaced Mr. De Merre as General
Manager. Mr. Umba is assisted by a Deputy General Manager, (presently vacant)
eight Managers, and three advisors including Mr. Kort (technical secretary),
Mr. Sorel (Production) and Mr. Rocher (Finance). With the exception of
Mr. Rocher who joined GECAMINES in 1971 when the Finance Department was
established in Lubumbashi, all managers have been with the Company for many
years. GECAMINES' technical staff appears to be competent, experienced and
compares favorably with the staff of other international mining companies.



ANNEX 2-3
Page 3
10.       The Company has pursued an active Africanization program on the
staff level.  When GECAMINES was formed in 1967, Zairian salaried staff
amounted to no more than 11.5% of total staff, whereas in 1973 African staff
numbered 923 out of a total of 2,427 (38%).

Africans
However, Tables 1 and 2 of Annex 2-7 indicate that
(i)  only 5% of all doctors and engineers are African compared
to a Company average of 37%, and that
(ii)  Africans are primarily employed for general and administrative
services. For example, Africans account for about 70% of the
staff in personnel administration and about 50% of staff em-
ployed for social services and general administration, while
only 20% of the mining staff and 27% of staff in the metallurgical
plants are Africans.
11.       GECAMINES is actively recruiting and training Zairian personnel.  The
Company maintains close contact with local and foreign universities, in partic-
ular engineering schools. However, while sufficient graduates in the social
sciences are available in Zaire, a continuing lack of Zairian engineers impedes
the Africanization of GECAMINES' production facilities. It is thus essential
that a national policy be developed to direct capable young people into tech-
nical careers and that the corresponding university facilities be encouraged.
Table 2 in Annex 2-7 illustrates the objective of GECAMINES' Zairization
program: The Company expects to employ a staff of 2,884 people in 1981 of
which 64% would be African.



ANNEX 2-3
Page 4
12.       Staff training within GECAMINES is directed by the "Division de la
Promotion Professionnelle et de l'Organisation". Staff training has been
progressive, and is an excellent example of integration of national personnel
into a corporate set-up. It is mainly of four forms:
(i) Trainee-ship outside of GECAMINES, primarily in Europe.
These trainee assignments have been organized by "Bureau
d'Etudes et de Documentation Sociales (B.E.D.S.) in Belgium.
Thirty trainees were sent abroad in 1972.
(ii) Scholarships for employees and their children to attend
educational institutions on the technical, secondary and
university level. 302 such scholarships have been awarded
in 1972.
(iii) Seminars for Junior staff in Lumbumbashi, and management
seminars in Kinshasa organized by IRI under the sponsorship
of the "Institut de Gestion du Portefeuille."
(iv)  Audio-visual language instruction.
13.       GECAMINES' recruitment policy foresees hiring of expatriates only
for jobs which require more than 3 years of experience or highly spcialized
training. Following the April 1974 agreements with SGM, all foreign personnel
have a direct contractual relationship with GECAMINES without SGM's back-up
employment guarantees. In the past, the majority of expatriate staff worked
for the Company for a life time, or at least for an extended period of time.
In the future, GECAMINES will experience a higher turnover which should even-
tually approach the job rotation pattern generally experienced by international
companies operating in developing countries, i.e., a turnover of 10-15%.
GECAMINES lost a number of expatriate staff since nationalization. This loss
was only partially overcome by hiring Zairian and temporary personnel. Never-
theless, the technical complexity of the GECAMINES operations will continue
to attract competent and motivated expatriate staff from french speaking coun-
tries. However, the availability of such personnel will depend on the politi-
cal stability in Zaire, the pace of Africanization and, therefore, the career
prospects of expatriates. In addition, recent difficulties in obtaining
working visas for foreigners should be eliminated.
14.       Expatriate staff is hired by SGM's Recruitment Department in Belgium
which requires an average of 6 months to find suitable staff for GECAMINES.
To supplement staff vacancies or recruit for special tasks, GECAMINES engages
professionals on a 6 - 24 month basis. At present the Company employs about
200 temporary staff, primarily engineers from supplier companies. Regarding
future expatriate personnel, a turnover of 9.5% for full-time staff members
and 50% for temporary staff is expected. Taking into account such turnover
and the present vacancies, about 100-250 expatriates per year will be required
until 1976, and annually 80 people thereafter.

Technical Assistance
15.       An agreement was reached in April 1974 between the Government of
Zaire, GECAMINES and SGM which adopted a new framework for SGM's technical
assistance to GECAMINES. Accordingly, SGM will make available to GECAMINES
or undertake at the latter's demand:
a)   purchasing services; in particular, surveying of suppliers,
contract negotiation, technical control, transport and payment
arrangements of equipment, materials and supplies.
b)   any studies relating to GECAMINES activities, in particular
operations of its production facilities, procurement of equip-
ment and documentation.
c)   personnel administration assistance-non-Zairian personnel; primarily
recruitment, organization of visits and trainee-ships, insurance
and retirement benefits, assistance during home leave.
16.       Remuneration of these activities is based on actual expenses as
outlined in an annual budget agreed upon by both partners. SCM in Brussels
maintains a division responsible for the GECAMINES activities. During 1974
SGM expects to render the following services, estimated at US$6.5 million:

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
Existing Operations and Technical Facilities
A. INTRODUCTION
1.        Gecamines' production facilities are grouped in three geographical
areas--the Kolwezi area (Western group), the Likasi region (Central group),
and the Lubumbashi area (Southern group). The distances from Lubumbashi to
Likasi and Kolwezi are 125 km and 300 km, respectively.
2.        Gecamines also treats different types of ores--dolomitic oxide,
siliceous oxide, mixed, and sulphide ores. The usual sequence for production
of copper from oxide ores involves: mining of ore; concentrating the economic
minerals utilizing their physical characteristics; hydro-metallurgical treat-
ment of the concentrate by an acid leach to dissolve the copper and other
metals; electro-winning whereby the metals in solution are deposited selectively
by electrolysis on cathodes; and finally refining tbe cathodes to obtain rela-
tively pure metal. For sulphide ores, the sequence is: mining of the ore;
concentration of the economic minerals; smelting of the concentrate to obtain
copper anodes; refining of the anodes, usually by electrolysis, to obtain rela-
tively pure metal.
3.        The geographical separation and different treatment processes results
in a complex production flowsheet (see Annex 2-4, page 11). For the sake of
clarity, this description of Gecamines has been presented by geographical group
so that the production sequence can be followed through the various steps to
pure metal.
4.        Gecamines prepares yearly, five year, and ten year plans to ensure
sufficient feed to the various plants, and that the Company's objectives are
met. All the facilities operate at a high percentage of rated capacity. The
plants and equipment are well maintained. The older facilities have continu-
ously been mdified and up-dated. As a result, most of the plants yield good
metal recoveries.
5.        This description of Gecamines' operations is based on the mission's
visits to most of the facilities, and on data supplied by Gecamines.
B. WESTERN GROUP
6.        The ore deposits in the western group are of the stratiform type.
The general description of the geology given below for the Kamoto mine is
typical of the deposits in the area.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 2
7.        Most of the expansion in the company's production has been centered
in the west. It is also the location of the proposed expansion to 590,000 t.
of copper. 75% of Gecamines' ore is mined and concentrated within the Western
Group. Six open pit mines and one underground mine, one washing plant and
two concentrators, treat some 11,100,000 t of ore per annum.
1.   Mines
Kamoto Underground Mine
8.        The ore zone consists of two mineralized strata (each 12 meters
thick) separated by a barren layer (12 to 20 m thick), and is basin shaped.
The overall length is approximately 1,500 m. The strata dip north at 250 -
30° in the western section with the dip gradually increasing to 700 - 900 in
the eastern portion. Below 350 m, the ore zone becomes virtually flat.
9.        The upper part of the ore zone was mined by open pit methods, and
development for underground mining commenced in 1959. Five shafts service
the underground - No. 1 shaft is used for production, No. 2 for personnel
and material, No. 3 for development work below the 307m level, and Nos. 4
and 5 for ventilation.
10.       In the flatter western portion of the ore zone, sublevel caving has
been used. This method is being superseded by transversal mechanized cut and
fill. The eastern part is mined by longitudinal meehanized cut and fill.
The mine produced 2,010,000 tons of mixed ore in 1973. Output will be ex-
panded to 3,000,000 t by 1977.
11.       The mine utilizes large, modern underground equipment.  The operation,
designed and operated on a sound engineering basis, compares favorably with
similar mines in the world. The equipment and facilities are well maintained.
Open Pit Mining
12.       The open pit mining in the western group can be divided into three
areas - Kamoto, Musonoi, and Mutoshi. The open pit operations are quite
similar throughout the company and the equipment and methods show little
variation. The oxide ores are relatively soft and do not usually require
blasting. Following extraction of the ore to the economic depth, the pits
are backfilled with waste from nearby pits.
Kamoto
13.       Kamoto.  There are presently three active pits in this area, Kamoto
East, Kamoto North and Mupine. These mines produce approximately 4,500,000 t
annually. Conventional equipment - 6, 9, 15 cu yd shovels, 45 and 100 t
trucks and 9-7/8" drills, is used.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 3
14.       Musonoi  Lying immediately to the east of the Kamoto pits, these
two pits supply 950,000 t annually. The Musonoi and Kamoto pits are connected
to a central dispatching unit to optimize use of equipment. The dispatching
unit also records the tonnages extracted, and waste removed.
15.       Mutoshi.  W4hile this pit does supply some 500,000 tons per annum of
typical ore, the main production is 2,700,000 t of breccia ore grading 1.50%
copper. This breccia ore consists of chunks of malachite in a clay matrix.
The stripping ratio (over the next ten years) is 2 waste to 1 ore; consider-
ably lower than other Gecamines' pits. The waste is removed by 15 c.y.
shovels and 100 t trucks. The ore is extracted by draglines and loaded in
rail cars for delivery to the Mutoshi washing plant.
2.   Concentration Plants
16.       Mutoshi Washing Plant.  The breccia ore, after washing and screening,
is treated by gravity separators (jigs) to produce a copper concentrate of
the heavier particles. The jig tailings are screened, with the oversize treated
in a heavy media separation and the fines (-1 mm) shipped to the Kolwezi con-
centrator for retreatment.
17.       Kolwezi Concentrator.  Now treating oxyde and mixed ores in different
sections of the plant during different periods of the year, the concentrator
will be used exclusively for oxyde ores following construction of the new con-
centrator. The feed to the oxyde circuit comes from the Musonoi, Kamoto, and
Mupine pits, and the fines from the Mutoshi washing plant. The oxide ores,
following crushing and grinding, are treated by flotation using a mixture
of palm oil and fuel oil as a collector and frother. 4,600,000 t of ore are
treated annually in this concentrator. Starting in 1977, the concentrator
will treat 2,000,000 of lower grade ore previously stockpiled.
18.       Kamoto Concentrator.  The newest concentrator in the Gecamines group,
Kamoto treats 3,600,000 tons of ore per year. The first section was completed
in late 1968, and the other in mid-1972. Both sections treat mixed ores al-
though Section II has larger, more modern equipment and a slightly different
flowsheet. After crushing and grinding, the mixed ores are treated by dif-
ferential flotation. The sulphides are floated first by the addition of
reagents to collect the sulphides. Other reagents are then added to alter
the physical characteristics of the minerals and float the oxides. The sulphide
concentrates are re-treated to produce a copper rich-cobalt poor concentrate
and a cobalt rich concentrate. The cobalt rich concentrates are pumped to
the Liulu Hydrometallurgical plant for further processing. The copper rich
concentrates are shipped to the Lubumbashi smelter.
3.   Metallurgical Plants
Luilu Hydrometallurgical Plant
19.       Production was initiated in 1960 with a capacity of 100,000 tons of
copper per annum. Expansion and modifications resulted in a capacity of
175,000 t of copper and 10,000 t of cobalt in 1973.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 4
20.       The sulphide ores are oxidized in a fluo-solid roaster to produce
sulphates and oxides of copper. These concentrates, together with the
siliceous oxide concentrates are then treated in a sulphuric acid primary
leaching circuit to obtain the copper, cobalt and other metals in solution.
21.       Thickening and clarification separates the solution of dissolved
metals from the sands. The sands are washed and filtered to remove all the
copper in solution, and then are discarded. Part of the solution is treated
to adjust the pH which removes some of the iron.  The solution is also fed to
selenium removal units.
22.       The solution, containing 17 to 20 g/l of H2SO4 and 65 to 70 g/l of
copper, is fed to the Electro-winning circuit where the copper is deposited
on cathode starter sheets. The depleted solution containing 60-70 g/l of
H2SO4 and 28 to 34 g/l of copper is returned to the primary leaching circuit,
with some 5% of the solution bled off to a secondary leaching circuit.
23.       The cathode starter sheets are prepared in a special section of
the electrolysis circuit. A solution (also from the thickening and clari-
fication section but containing 150 g/l of H2SO4 an d 45 g/l of copper) is
electrolysed under controlled conditions to produce sheets with a satisfactory
structure and which can be removed without tearing.
24.       The secondary leaching circuit is used to treat dolomitic oxide
concentrates. The high acid consumption of the dolomites is utilized to
neutralize the excess acid in the solution. Otherwise the process is as out-
lined above for the primary circuit. The solution is treated by electrolysis
and the depleted solution containing 22-28 g/l of H2SO4 and 10-15 g/l of copper
is returned to the secondary leaching circuit.
25.       Some of the depleted solution from the secondary circuit is bled
off to form the feed to the cobalt circuit. Following treatment of the solution
to remove the balance of the copper, and other metals (iron, zinc, nickel,
etc.) by selective precipitation the solution is too weak for proper cobalt
electrolysis. The cobalt is, therefore, precipitated and re-dissolved. This
solution is then treated by electrolysis to recover the cobalt. This cobalt
(in the form of small plates) is then refined in vacuum furnaces to remove
entrained hydrogen and produce high quality cobalt metal.
4.   Kolwezi Zinc Plant (UZK)
26.       Formed in 1948 as a subsidiary of Belgian interests, the company
(known as Metalkat) commenced production in 1953 with a capacity of 48,000 t
of zinc metal. Expansion and modifications have resulted in a plant with
a capacity of 72,000 t. As of 1 January 1974, DMetalkat has been absorbed
into Gecamines as a wholly owned subsidiary.
27.       Prior to treatment in the Kolwezi Zinc Plant, the zinc concentrates
(from the Kipushi min'e in the Southern Group) are roasted at the UCS sulphuric
acid plant at Likasi. The roasting oxidizes the sulphide minerals and yields
sulphur dioxide gas for the production of sulphuric acid.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 5
28.       These roasted concentrates are then subjected to leaching at the
zinc plant to obtain the zinc in solution. Other metals (copper, cadmium,
arsenic, cobalt) in varying quantities are also dissolved. Following treat-
ment of the solution to selectively precinitate these metals, it is then
treated by electrolysis to deposit the zinc. This cathode zinc is melted
in induction furnaces and cast as zinc ingots. Some zinc powder is also
produced.
29.       The precipitates mentioned above are used to produce copper cement
(94% copper) and cadmium metal. The cadmium is produced in a special
electrolytic section, melted and casted as sticks.
C. CENTRAL GROUP
30.       The mines in the Central Group are located near the town of Likasi.
They presently produce some 1,500,000 t of ore per annum, which is treated
in the Kambove and Kakanda concentrators. In addition, the Kambove concen-
trator has been re-treating some 600,000 t of low grade ore stockpiled during
mining of the M'sesa and Kazibizi open pits.
31.       The ore deposits in the Central Group are also of the stratiform
type. The geology is similar to that described for the mines of the western
group.
1.   Mines
Kambove Underground Mine
32.       The upper portion of the ore zone was mined by open pit until 1965.
Production from underground commenced in 1963. Three shafts service the
underground -- No. 1 shaft is the production, No. 2 the personnel and materials,
No. 3, ventilation.
33        Since 1968, the sublevel caving method of mining has been used.
Although the method has permitted high and expanding levels of production,
it does have the disadvantage of a high percentage dilution.
34.       It is expected to increase production from approximately 800,000 t
to 1,400,000 t per year by 1975.
Open Pit Mining
35.       There are presently two active open pits in the Central Group -- the
Kakanda East and Kakanda North. Economically exploitable reserves by surface
mining are expected to be exhausted by 1977. The production will be replaced
by the expansion in output from Kambove mine and exploitation of previously
stock piled mixed ore of the Kazibizi open pit. Production has been approxi-
mately 800,000 t per 'year for the past few years.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 6
36.       The method of mining is similar to that used in the open pits in
the Western Group. 6, 9, 15 cu. yd. shovels, 100 t trucks, 9-7/8" drills, are
the main equipment items used.
2.   Concentrators
37.       Kambove.  Constructed in 1961 with a capacity of 860,000 tons per
year, the concentrator has been expanded to 1,400,000 tons per year. The
plant is thus reasonably up to date, and it has been well maintained. Mineral
dressing techniques are good -- yielding good mineral recoveries considering
the types of ore treated. The concentrator treats mixed ores, and oxide ores
during different periods of the year. The flowsheet is similar to that of the
Kolwezi concentrator. Kambove will be used exclusively for treatment of the
mixed ores from the Kambove mine after 1975.
38.       Kakanda.  Constructed in 1962 with a capacity of 300,000 t of ore,
the Kakanda concentrator has also undergone expansion to a capacity of
720,000 by 1965. The concentrator is comparable to the Kambove concentrator
in design and equipment. It is used to treat oxide ores using the palm oil
method. The concentrator is presently supplied from the Kakanda East and
Kakanda North open pits. After 1977, the concentrator will be modified to
treat mixed ores.
3.   MIetallurgical Plants
Shituru Hydrometallurgical Plant
39.       The Shituru plant, at Likasi, was constructed in 1929 at a capacity
of 35,000 t of copper per year. The plant was originally built to treat
oxide ores by acid leaching. Hlowever, by 1932, oxide concentrates were used
exclusively. Expansion and modification have resulted in a plant similar
to that at Luilu. Capacity is presently 135,000 t per year of copper and
6,000 t of cobalt.
40.       Although the plant is old, it has been exceptionally well maintained.
The Shituru plant operates at a high percentage availability. It is not as
efficient as the Luilu plant, and does not have the modern process control
used at Luilu. Recoveries are also showing a slight decline. However, all
considered, the operation of the Shituru plant is extremely good.
41.       As indicated above, the process is similar to that of the Luilu
plant. The technology, being less modern, does result in products of lower
quality. The difference in the copper is very slight. However, the cobalt
cathodes are 94% Co and must be fire-refined to remove some of the metallic
impurities and sulphur. The granules produced are shipped to Luilu for
further refining, or are sold.
42.       The Shituru plant also includes a copper refinery.  The cathode
copper from electro-winning (99.5% Cu) is refined in reverberatory furnaces
(99.95% Cu) and cast as wire-bar or ingot copper. One of the furnaces is
used to produce anodes for use in electro-winning.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 7
D. SOUTHERN GROUP
43.       Site of some of first mining in Shaba province, the facilities in
the Southern Group are among the oldest in Gecamines. However, these
facilities are being modernized on a systematic basis. The existing facilities
are in excellent condition and yield good metallurgical recoveries. The
company's executive, administrative, and research facilities are also located
in Lubumbashi, center of the Southern Group.
1.   Kipushi Underground Mine
44.       The oldest producing mine in Gecamines, Kipushi commenced production
from underground in 1926. As outlined in the geology annex, it is also the
only vein type deposit being mined. Production is presently at 1,090,000 tons
of copper-zinc ore per year.
45.       The principal mining method, top slicing, is comparably labor in-
tensive, and difficult to modernize. One major improvement has been the
introduction of steel support and mats, eliminating the fire hazard of wood
which had plagued the operation.
46.       The satellite zones are mined by sublevel stoping.  These zones
are small and quite irregular, but the method is well adapted to the condi-
tions.
47.       As part of the first expansion plan (1969-74), a new shaft (#5)
was sunk to develop the deeper portion of the ore zone. This work is to
be completed by 1975. The mine will also be converted to mechanized cut and
fill, and output expanded to 1,400,000 tons of ore. This change should in-
crease percentage extraction and lower mining costs.
2.   Concentration Plants
Kipusiti
48.       Originally constructed in 1935 at a capacity of 250,000 t of ore
per year, the Kipushi concentrator used simple flotation followed by gravity
separation. At this stage, only copper was recuperated.
49.       After considerable research, a method of differential flotation
was developed to produce copper concentrates, zinc concentrates (and period-
ically), lead concentrate. The plant actually has two circuits -- one for
copper-zinc ores, and the other for zinc rich ores. Capacity is presently
1,100,000 tons of ore per year.
50.       The present bottleneck in the operation -- the crushing plant --
will be eliminated with the start-up of the new underground facilities.
Following crushing underground, the ore will be hoisted to the grinding
plant near the shaft and pumped to the present flotation circuit. The grind-
ing plant will be equipped with autogenous grinding mills. The plant capacity
will then be 1,400,000 tons of ore per year.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 8
3.   Metallurgical Plants
Lubumbashi Smelter
51.       Originally placed in operation in 1911 for the treatment of oxide
ores, the smelter has evolved over the years into a classic smelting operation.
Presently the bulk of the feed consists of copper concentrates from Kipushi
and copper rich concentrates from the Western Group.
52.       The concentrates are first roasted in a Dwight Lloyd roaster.  This
is followed by smelting with the addition of rich concentrates, silica,
converter slag, etc. in water jacket furnaces to produce a copper matte.
The matte is fed to a converter for production of blister copper (at 98% Cu).
The blister is cast into 150 kg ingots.
53.       Periodically, the plant is used to treat oxides to produce a "Black
copper" (94% Cu). During these periods, the "mud" and nodules from electro-
winning and slag from refining at Shituru are added to the circuit.  The
process - roasting, smelting -- is basically the same, although there is
no convertor operation. The additives are, of course, different.
54.       Improvements during the last expansion program increased the
capacity from 130,000 t of copper to 165,000 t. These included construction
of an oxygen plant to permit enrichment of the air used in the furnaces and
converters. A pelletizing unit also permitted addition of rich concentrates
directly to the converter.
55.       Although some of the equipment is quite old, it is expected that
further modifications during the next expansion program will result in a
modern plant.
E.   Research Facilities
1.   Mining Studies and Research
56.       The mining research facilities are located at Lubumbashi.  Applied
research in the fields of operations research and rock mechanics represent the
principal activities of the section.
57.       In operations research, the studies undertaken range from optimizing
the production schedule of the treatment plant in view of mining constraints;
thru underground design and scheduling; to equipment studies. Studies are
scheduled in relation to the possible overall benefit to the Company.
58.       Work in the rock mechanics section has been concerned mainly with
slope stability and design in the open pit mines. Laboratory and field studies
have also been of the mining methods used in the Kamoto underground mine.



ANNEX 2-4
Page 9
2.   Metallurgical Studies and Research
59.       The metallurgical research facilities are located at Likasi.  The
group is divided into six sections--mineral dressing, pyrometallurgical,
hydrometallurgical, operations research, engineering, and analytical labora-
tories.
60.       The mineral dressing section deals with the physical concentration
of the ore. This has covered, mainly, the use of reagents and pilot studies
for concentration of new ore deposits.
61.       The pyro-metallurgical section has been concerned with development
and testing of the flash smelting process for the expansion unit.
62.       The hydro-metallurgical section is currently working on pilot tests
for solvent extraction of the solutions from the leaching circuits. The aim
is to upgrade the solutions sufficiently to produce electro-grade copper in
the electro-winning circuits.
63.       All three sections have well equipped laboratories.  In addition to
the specific studies indicated above, they routinely study areas of the exist-
ing operations as required.
64.       The operations research section undertakes studies on optimization
of production, costing of new projects, treatment plant design, etc.
65.       The engineering section has worked on such studies as:  development
and testing of equipment; improvement of wear parts, etc. It also assists in
selection of standardized equipment, conveyor belts, for example.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
GEOLOGY AND ORE RESERVES
A.   Geolpgy
1.         Gecamines holds concession rights over an 18,000 km2 (300 km long
by 60 km wide) in southern Shaba province (see Map IBRD No. 11155).  Commonly
known as the Copper Concession, the rights cover all minerals which may occur.
The company also holds concession rights (known as the Tin Concession) to a
larger area to the north of the Copper Concession. However, little work has
been done on this concession.
2.         Descriptions of the geology can be found in the following reports:
1.   La Cuvette orientale du Haut Katanga, A. Timmerhans, Ann. Soc.
Geol. Bel. T LIV, pp. 71-87, 1930.
2.   La Tectonique des gites de cuivre du Katanga, M. Schulling,
Centenaire d'A. I. Lg. Congres 1947, Sect. Col. pp. 309-313, 1947.
3.   Geologie et Geographie du Katanga, M. Robert, Brussels, 1956.
4.   The Precambrian of the Congo, Rwanda, and Burundi, L. Cahen and
J. Lepersonne, The Precambrian, Vol. 3, p. 143, 1967.
5.   L'Extreme Occidentale de L'Arc Cuprifere Shabien-etude geologique,
A. Francois, Gecamines, 1973.
3.         There are two types of ore within the Copper Concession.  The most
important are the sedimentarv or stratiform deposits which make up the bulk
of the ore reserves. The other is a vein type deposit (found in the southern
part of the concession) of the copper-zinc ore. These deposits represent some
of the world's highest grade copper reserves.
4.         The stratiform ore occurs within two specific formations in the
Roan series of the sedimentary system known locally as Katangian. The upper
formation is a sandy shale containing some carbonates. The lower formation
is a bedded dolomitic sandstone. Separating the two formations is siliceous
dolomite some 13 to 22 meters in thickness. The ore formations average
approximately ten meters in thickness. While these formations within the
Roan series are not always mineralized, the major ore deposits do occur with-
in this series.
5.         The structural geology of the ore deposits is quite complex.  Three
different ages of tectonic activity have been identified. As a result, the
ore formations are heavily folded, and are often interrupted by faults. In
some areas, they are not present, having been eroded away or otherwise dis-
placed.



ANNEX 2-5
Page 2
6.        The most common copper mineral is Chalcocite (essentially CuS2).
The cobalt usually occurs as Carrolite (Co2CuS4). Accessory elements include
selenium, nickel, gold and silver.
7.        The sulphide mineralization has been altered by weathering in those
portions of the ore zones close to surface. This weathering varies consider-
ably in depth from one deposit to the other (80 metres to 450 metres). As a
result, the sulphide minerals have been altered to carbonates (malachite),
silicates (chryscolla, Katangite, Bisbecite) and phosphates (Pseudomalachite,
Libethenite).
8.        The alteration has also resulted in important supergenetic movements.
The ore formations may be completely leached out, whereby the mineralization
has disappeared or been concentrated in normally sterile formations. On the
other hand, econcomic concentrations of oxidized minerals may be derived from
low grade sulphide mineralization.
9.        There is only one known economic vein deposit within the Copper
Concession, the Kipushi, which is in the southern group.  It consists of
massive sulphides within the dolomites known locally as Kakontwe, along the
Kipushi fault. It has been classed as a metasomatic hydrothermal deposit.
Three distinct types of zones are mined-copper rich, zinc rich, and mixed
copper-zinc. The principal minerals are chalcopyrite, bornite, cadmiumiferous
sphalerite, galena, and pyrite. Accessory minerals&include Arsenopyrite,
Tennantite.
10.       With the stratiform type of ore deposit, geological mapping is of
the utmost importance in the discovery of new ore deposits. Gecamines'
program of mapping is proceeding in a systematic well planned fashion. The
whole of the concession area has been mapped on a 1:200,000 scale. In
addition, Gecamines has a well developed program of geological mapping on a
scale of 1:20,000. A significant portion of this program (approximately 50%)
has been completed.
11.       In areas identified as likely to be of economic importance, maps
at scales of 1:5,000 and/or 1:1,000 are prepared. Some diamond drilling may
also be undertaken to further evaluate the deposit. The deposit would then
be placed on the schedule for detailed evaluation. The timing of this
detailed evaluation depends on the company's needs and priorities. For example,
one aim is to supply the concentrators, smelters, etc. with ore from within
that group to minimize ore transport.
12.       The Company has also surveyed the copper concession with airborne
magnetic and radiometric surveys and has within the past few years, undertaken
an extensive program of geochemical surveys.
B.   Ore Reserves
13.       Measured ore reserves in the Copper Concession total 646 million
tons of 3.95% copper and 0.3% cobalt for the stratiform ore; and 10 million
tons of 4.9% copper and 11.8% zinc for the vein type deposit. The potential



ANNEX 2-5
Page 3
reserves, in both cases, are probably of the order of twice the measured
reserves. The company has sufficient measured reserves to last more than 40
years at the projected rate of mining.
14.       The ore reserves are conservatively estimated, with allowances for
dilution, sampling error, and cut-off grade based on experience.
15.       65% of the known reserves are located in the Western group of the
Concession. Approximately 75% of the presently known reserves are estimated
to be mineable by open pit methods.
16.       The reserve estimates have been verified and discussed with the
Company. Since they represent confidential information, no further details
are presented here.
Industrial Projects Department
July 1974

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
INFRASTRUCTURE
A.   Transport
1.        There are two types of transport to consider:  (i) Internal trans-
port of Gecamines materials at various stages of processing; and (ii) Transport
of exported products from Shaba province to coastal ports. In the first case,
Gecamines with KDL a state owned railway operating in Southern Zaire, are
responsible. In the second case, four alternate routes, each with multiple
carriers, are used to transport Gecamines' exports.
1.   Internal Transport
2.        The distances between the three groups (Western, Central, and
Southern) and transfer of material for further processing results in exten-
sive requirements for material movement.
3.        Gecamines has its own locomotives and cars for movement of ores,
concentrates, and other materials within each site. The movement of material
from site to site and group to group is provided by KDL.
4.        Gecamines owns ore cars of the bottom dump type, and fixed box
type. Some of these are quite old but have been well maintained. All loco-
motives owned by Gecamines are in a good state of repair. Normal maintenance
is performed at the maintenance facilities located in the Central and Uestern
group, on a regularly scheduled basis.
5.        With the increased use of pipelines for transport of concentrates,
and the good condition of the equipment and rail lines, no transport problems
should be encountered in the expansion of output. In effect, the movement
of materials in the expansion project creates little additional load on the
rail transport system. The bulk of the material will be moved by other
means -- pipeline, conveyor, etc.  The major additions are the 120,000 t of
copper exports, 75,000 tons of lignite, and 25,000 t of limestone.
2.   External Tragryt
6.        Four rail transportation routes (see IBRD 'tap #11156) are available
for export of Gecamines products. This number of routes offers more flexibi-
lity than is found in most mining projects.



ANiiLX 2-6
Page Z
7.        An assessment of each of the four routes indicates that difficulties
could be encountered on each of the lines. In addition, political decisions
in neighbouring countries could affect exports to an unknown extent on three
of the routes. The four routes ship products via Matadi, Dar-es-Salaam, Beira
and Lobito.
a)   Matadi (Voie Nationale)
8.        This all Zairian route is composed of the KDL rail line to Ilebo,
river barge on the Kasai and Zaire river from Ilebo to Kinshasa, and rail
line from Kinshasa to the port of Matadi. The river and rail link are operated
by ONATRA, a state owned organization. The total distance is 2,750 km and
requires 20 to 25 days transport time.
9.        Gecamines is required by the government to export 270,000 tons of
copper by this route. Ilowever, actual shipments have fallen short of this
due to constraints on the river portion of the route. In 1973, 240,000 tons
were actually shipped. ONATRA, in a Bank financed project, expects to increase
capacity on the river route, with 300,000 tons available for copper by 1977.
Supply of the casting plant to be constructed in Bas, Zaire near Kinshasa will
be via this route. The plant will have an initial capacity of 100,000 t in
1978, to be expanded to 200,000 t in the early 1980's.
10.       The major problems to be expected on this route involve the river
portion. Delays of transshipment are frequent, and some losses of cargo have
been experienced. However, the ONATRA project includes provision for impioved
handling facilities for copper products. Delays should be minimized as a
result.
b)   Dar-es-Salaam
11.       The four components of this route are the KDL rail line to Kabongo,
the CFL rail line to Kalemie, CFL barge across Lake Tanganika, and the East
African Railway line to Dar-es-Salaam. CFL is a Zaire state-owned railway.
Total distance is 2,715 km and transport time is 20 to 25 days.
12.       The government requires shipment of 100,000 tons of copper on this
route. Constraints in the port of Dar-es-Salaam limit shipments to approxi-
mately 80,000 tons of copper each year. The port of Dar-es-Salaam is extremely
overloaded due to Zambia's decision to close its border to Rhodesia. The
bulk of Zambia's imports and exports (including copper) flows through Dar-es-
Salaam. Until this port is expanded, shipments of Gecamines copper will not
exceed the present level.
c)   Beira
13.       This all rail route is 2,615 km long and transport time is 15 to
20 days.  Carriers are KDL to Sabania, Zambian Railways to Livingstone,
Rhodesian Railways to Umtali, and CFM in Mozambique to Beira. Gecamines
exports through Beira were 30,000 tons in 1973. While shipments could be



ANNEX 2-6
Page 3
much higher (of the order of 150,000 tons), Gecamines current policy is to
match shipments with imports of metallurgical coal from Wankie in Rhodesia.
d)   Lobito
14.       The most direct and shortest of routes available to Gecamines,
this route is 2,110 km long. Transport time is 10 to 12 days. Carriers are
KDL to Dilolo and Benguela railways in Angola to the port of Lobito. Gecamines
exported all of its cobalt (16,000 tons) and 120,00 tons of copper through
Lobito in 1973. Most of Gecamines' imports are handled through Lobito.
Presently Lobito absorbs the balance of Gecamines exports. More could be
handled, with the quantity depending on the level of Zambian copper exports
through Lobito.
15.       The declaration by Zambia in mid-1974 that all its copper exports
would be shipped through Lobito is an example of the effect of political deci-
sions. This declaration resulted from a decision by.the Tanzanian government
to increase very substantially the rail and port handling charges in Dar-es-Salaam
for Zambian copper. This particular problem will probably be solved following
negotiations between Tanzanian and Zambian authorities. However, it is an indi-
cation of the difficulty of assessing the transport situation for Gecamines.
16.       Political considerations excluded, sufficient capacity exists to
handle Gecamines increased production. Additional capacity on the Matadi
line, and spare capacity on the Beira and Lobito lines more than cover the
planned increase in production by Gecamines and other copper producers in
Zaire.
17.       The tariffs on all four routes are degressive.  The rates and incre-
mental decreases, outlined in Annex 6-1, vary with the route.
B.   Power Sup_ply
18.       The supply of electrical energy must be regarded as a critical
element both in the expansion module and for maintenance of present production.
The capacity of the hydroelectric plants in Shaba province was reached in 1973.
Through an exchange agreement with Zambia, Zaire is assured of sufficient power
for Shaba until early 1975. Zairian power totalling 420 GWh was supplied to
Zambia during the period 1967-72. This is to be returned to Zaire at the same
cost. It is expected that the return will be completed by early 1975. Con-
struction of the 1,800 km power line from Inga in Bas Zaire to Shaba is pres-
ently expected to be completed in late 1977. Therefore the period 1975 to
1977 is not covered by an assured supply. SNEL (Societe Nationale d'Electricite)
will sign a supply contract with Gecamines for t' is period, and could, by re-
allocation among its customers, meet Gecamines' rreeds.  Gecamines also ca.
redistribute available slpplies from power intensive zinc prodiuetion to copper
production, witlh only marginally lower cash generation.

19.       In addition to this projected demand, SMTF is expected to commence
production in 1978 at 125,000 tons of copper.  Electrical consumption is
projected at 495 GWh increasing to 625 GWh in 1980.
20.       At present, electrical power is supplied from four hydroelectric
installations owned by SNEL. Two of these, Mwandingusha and Koni are located
on the Lufira river and are fed by the Mwadingusha reservoir. The other
two, Nzilo and Seke, are located on the Lualaba river and fed by the Delcommue
reservoir. These two reservoirs are relatively small. The low rainfall during
the 1973-74 rainy season has caused some drawdown of the reservoirs. Any
future shortfall in precipitation could adversely affect electrical production,
particularly in 1976.
21.       Power is supplied to GECAMINES from these installations through 120
KV lines to substations located in each of GECAMINES groups of installations --
Western, Central, and Southern. Inter-ties exist between the three groups.
There is also a 220 KV line from the Seke dam to Zambia which is connected to
the Central and Southern groups.
22.       Present power consumption represents 3,870 KWh per ton of copper
with an average price of 0.0049 Z per KWh. Consumption for the expansion
module is estimated to be 285 GWh. While the price of power from Inga has
not been determined as yet, a price of 0.008 Z per KWh has been used for
the financial projections.
C.   Water Supply
23.       Water is currently supplied from streams, and pumping from the
mines. GECAMINES is responsible for locating and exploiting its water
supplies. To date, no difficulties have been experienced.



ANNEX 2-6
Page 5
24.       GECAMINES operates water treatment plants to provide treated water
for industrial use. Treatment involves filtration for washing operations,
softening and demineralization for the electrolytic circuit, steam boilers,
and acid plant as well as drinking water.
25.       Water consumption for the expansion module is estimated at 3,400 m3
per hour. This will be supplied from the Luilu river. Expansion of the
existing pumping station and installation of another water treatment plant
are required for the supply of water.
26.       Cost of supply and treatment is estimated at 0.01 Z per m3.
D.   Workshops
27.       In addition to workshops at each plant site, Gecamines also has two
large, well equipped maintenance facilities. The large "Atelier Central" is
located at Likasi in the Central group, while the other "Atelier Ouest" services
the Western group operations.
28.       The workshops at each plant site offer essential maintenance and
repair services -- electrical, mechanical and carpentry. The Central mainte-
nance facilities provides a vast range of fabrication and maintenance services.
These include: (a) pattern shop; (b) foundry; (c) mechanical workshop; (d}
engine rebuilding shop; (e) garage; (f) electrical'workshop; and (g) electronic
workshop.
29.       The "Atelier Central" is well organized and well equipped.  However
some of the workshops and equipment could be modernized. Since most of the
shops have spare capacity, this is not a constraint on the services offered.
A total of 1,000 employees work in the Central maintenance facilities.
30.       The Western maintenance facilities were established to reflect the
growing production of the Western Group. It is intended to complement the
services offered by the "Atelier Central". The main facilities are the Garage
for maintenance of mining equipment and a mechanical workshop for recondition-
ing parts for mining and metallurgical equipment. Other facilities include
an electrical workshop, a radio communication repair shop, and a shop for
repair of hydraulic sub-assemblies.
31.       The "Atelier Ouest" is well organized and well equipped.  The
installations are generally newer than those of the "Atelier Central". Some
600 employees work here. Incorporated in these facilities is a training
school for electrical and mechanical maintenance.
32.       GECAMINES has a preventive maintenance program which covers all its
mechanical and electrical equipment.



ANNEX 2-6
Page 6
E.   Telecomuunications
33.       GECAMINES operates its own telephone system within its three groups
of operations. In addition, it has direct Telex communications with Kinshasa.
34.       In 1971, the telephone system was modernized.  This included the
installation of micro-wave connections between the three groups.
35.       Investments totaling 450,000 Z are projected to expand the Western
group central and increase connections to Lubumbashi. The telex connections
will also be increased.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
MANPOWER
A.   Labor Situation
1.        During the 1967 to 1973 period GECAMINES' copper production grew
by 55% from 319,000 tons to 495,500 tons, whereas the labor force increased
by only 17.5%. The apparent productivity increase required an upgrading of
the existing and newly hired labor force. Table 1, of this Annex shows that
the number of unskilled workers decreased by 5.2% compared to about a 60%
increase of semi-skilled labor and a 340% increase of skilled workers.
2.        GECAMINES maintains a stable labor force.  Unexcused absenteeism is
low, ranging between 2.5 - 3.0%. In 1972, GECAMINES hired 3,067 workers, while
1,690 workers left the Company, mainly because of insufficient skill level
(32%), retirement or death (28%) and contract termination (15%). The job
creation of the past and coming expansion programs results in an annual net
increase in labor as shown below and in Table 2 of this Annex.
Manpower Fluctuation

Loss            7.31       3.35      6.79       9.79       7.01       5.54
3.        Traditionally GECAMINES recruited and trained workers from Zaire,
Rwanda and other neighboring countries. These workers and their families
have been integrated into GECAMINES system of social services, and thus
have been offered a new stable environment. According to Zairian legislation
not more than 10% of workers are to be foreigners. GECAMINES has continuously
decreased its share of foreign workers in order to meet the legal target.

4.        All workers are members of the Zairian Labor Union (UNTZA) which
negotiates a labor contract every 2 years and periodocially reviews social
and educational arrangements as well as safety measures.
5.        Accident prevention has been a major part of GECAMINES' manpower
policy.  The Company's Safety Department pursues an active program to prevent
accidents at work by providing inspections and adequate design of work places,
good safety equipment and safety regulations coordinated with safety awareness
drives.
6.        Salartes of surface workers are at about the same level as wages in
the Kinshasa area.  However, only 35% of total labor cost accounts for cash
wages, but GECATINES workers benefit from special allowances and subsidies,
such as free housing, educational and health services.  CECMITNES costa data is
confidential, therefore no further details are Presented.  The cost data has
been reviewed withi the comnany (see nara. 9-11 below).
B.   Training and Education
7.        GECAMINES provides training of workers in the form of (i) introductory
and upgrading training on the job and (ii) instruction in primary, secondary
and technical schools. Training expenses account for about 6% of total labor
cost. Traditionally, GECAMINES has maintained excellent training programs
which are outstanding compared to the training efforts of other Zairian
organizations.
8.        On the job training including theoretical class-room instructions
ranges from 1-3 day courses to 3-6 month traineeships in several of GECAMINES
plants and mines. About one-third of the instructors are Zairian. The Com-
pany's training effort more than doubled since 1970 as indicated below:

C.   Social Facilities
10.       GECAMINES continues to provide a high level of social facilities.
The Company maintaines workers settlements so-called "cites" near the existing
mines and plants which offer housing, primary schools and other communal services
such as sports grounds, churches, administrative buildings, market places and
meeting halls. In 1972, GECAMINES owned about 20,749 housing units of 2 - 4
rooms each in 12 cites'. Another 2,330 houses are planned to be constructed as
part of the 1974-79 expansion project.
