ZAIRE: GECAJIINES EXPANSION PPOJECT
THE COPPER INDUSTRY IN ZAIRE
A.   INTRODUCTION
1.        The copper industry in Zaire operates since 1906 and is located
in Shaba at the southern end of the country in a belt 60 km wide and 300
km long. Zaire is the world's sixth largest producer of copper, after the
USA, USSR, Chile, Zambia, and Canada, accounting for about 8.07 of the
VTestern world mine production (Annex 3-2).
Copper mining in Shaba yields several by-products such as cobalt,
zinc, cadmium, germanium, silver and gold. Cobalt is the most valuable
hy-product with exports amounting to 13,200 tons in 1973 or about 58% of the
world's cobalt production.
2.        The Government of Zaire has given high nriority to the develop-
ment of the copper sector during the past 5 vears.  The authorities believe
that in the medium term an increase in mining would be the fastest most
reliable way to increase foreign exchange earnings and raise government
revenues.  Furthermore, investment in copper mining has been  particularly
sluggish in the immediate post-indenendence period, so that Zaire is now attempt-
in, to regain its earlier share in the international coDoer market. This nolicv
which should result in an increase of Zaire's mining and processing capacity
has been nursued on three different levels involving:
(i)  the expansion of GECA'TNES, the state-owned copper producing
comnany,
(ii)  the attraction of foreign private investment to set up new
mining companies in hitherto unexplored areas, and
(iii)  the construction of copper processing canacities in Zaire
which would increase the domestic value added and allow the
processing of 1007 of the country's coDper extraction in Zaire.
The latter targyet was announced by President Tfobutu in a
speech on November 30, 1973.
B.   COPPER MIINIHIG CO'fP;NIES
3.        For decades the Zairian copper industry was synonymous with sro-
duction by U'INIC, a..d later GECAMINES. After Independence the Government felt
that the absorptive capacity of one single company was insufficent to exploit the
country's vast and rich conper resources. Consequently, the Government of
Zaire has granted new exploration and mining rights to 2 comnanies --
SODIMIIZA (Societe de Develonnement Industriel et Mines du Zaire) and STF
(Societe "iniere de Tenke-Fungurume). SODIMIZA started production of
coDper concentrates in 1972. SMTF is exDected to come on stream in 1978.



ANNEX 3-1
Page 2
1.   GECAMINES
4.        GECAMINES, the old established copper mining company, at present
produces about 460,000 tons of copper, 16,000 tons of cobalt and 78,000 tons
of zinc.  GECAMINES holds concession rights to 18,000 sq. km which covers
most of the copper arc in Shaba. Measured reserves are sufficient for over
40 years at the present projected mining rate but the potential is at least
twice the published reserves. The Company's history, operating facilities,
financial structure, and organization are discussed in Annexes 2-1 to 2-9.
5.        Following the nationalization of UMHK and the creation of GECAMN.4ES
the management of Gecamines initiated an expansion program designed to increase
copper production from 364,000 tons in 1969 to 600,000 tons by 1980. In its
first expansion (1970-74) the Company expanded copper production to 460,000
tons in 1974 and cobalt production to about 16,000 tons p.a. Investment costs
for this first expansion Plan reached Z 130 million which were financed by
internal cash generation and loans totalling US$32 million from the US Eximbank
and the European Investment Bank.  In order to permit GECA-INES to finance
the project primarily by its owm resources the Government granted the company
special tax concessions under the Investment Code. The concession stipulates
that until 1974 GECAIINES will be exempt from the payment of all taxes on
annual production of copper exceeding 360,000 tons and of cobalt exceeding
11,000 tons as well as miscellaneous import duties and taxes related to the
expansion. The project evaluated in this report represents the second 5-Year
Development Plan (1974-79) and should allow GECA1-rTNES to reach its production
target for this decade (for details see Annexes 4-1 to 4-3).
6.        At present, GECAMINES refines about 230,000 tons of copper or more
than 50% of its copper output in Zaire (Annex 2-4, Table 1). The remaining
production consisting of cathode, blister and crude copper is processed
in Belgium.  GECAIINES will directlv refine in Shaba the additional 120,000
tons output generated by the second 5-Year Develonment Plan (1974-79) and
can decrease its supply of unrefined copper to SCM - Mletallurgie Hoboken by
50,000 tons per annum.after 1980.  fSee Annex 3-4).
7.        GECAMINES' relations with SC;G have been newly defined bY several
agreements signed on April 10, 1974. Thev intend to diminish Zaire's de-
pendence on Sr' and Belgium by regulating (i) finally the compensation
payments for Union Miniere assets, (ii) the marketing of Zairian mineral
products by a Zairian-owned company in Zaire instead of SGM", and defining
(iii) the technical assistance provided by SCM for GECAMINES.  Details are
given in Annex 3-4, paras. 5-6.
8.        Following President Mlobutu's announcement on November 30, 1974,
former subsidiaries of Union Mtiniere were finally nationalized. As a
result, 6 companies representing 8% of GECAN1INES net fixed asset were in-
corporated in GECAMINES (for details see Annex 2-2). The annexation of
these 6 subsidiaries improves GECAMINES forward linkages by adding IJZK
(,Ietalkat), a zinc processing company as well as horizontal linkages, since
UCS (SOGECHIMf), C1L and La Fonciere provide intermediate goods, supplies
and services to GECXAJINES.



ANNEX 3-1
Page 3
2.   SODIMIZA
9
SODIMIZA (Societe de Developpement Industriel et Mines du Zaire)
was established in April 1969 as a joint venture between the Government and
Japanese interests. The capital is owned 85% by CODIMICO, a consortium of
the Nippon Mining Company and other Japanese firms 1/, and the Government of
Zaire which holds 15% of the share capital.
10.       SODIMIZA holds concession rights to 36,790 sq. miles along the
Zambian border and south of the GECAMINFS concession near Kambove and Likasi.
After extensive exploration on the basis of GECAMINES' records and new aerial
and ground surveys, SODIMIZA developed in a first phase the Motoshi deposit
southeast of Lubumbashi. Ore reserves of Motoshi are estimated at 100 million
tons averaging 3.5% copper content. Production started in 1972 reaching 25,000
tons and will increase to its capacity of 50,000 tons in 1975. The facilities
in Mutoshi produce 30-36% copper concentrate which is exported to Japan for
smelting and refining.
11.       At present, SODIMIZA is exploring the Tshinsenda deposits near
the Zambian border. SODIMI7A has to decide within two years whether it
intends to exploit the deposit or otherwise the concession will revert
to the Government of Zaire. The opening of the Tshinsenda mine could yield
an additional 60,000 tons of copner per annum.
12.       The convention betwjeen the Nippon Mlining Company and the Government
of Zaire regulates the fiscal system, the marketing rights and processing
requirements as follows:
(a) The Government of Zaire can purchase within the first 5 years
50% of SODIMIZA's ownership;
(b)  Nippon Mining ComDany and SODIMIZA will spend nearly US$4
million in prospecting the concession area;
(c)  Nippon Mining Company has the right to buy the entire mine out-
put but has to install refining facilities in Zaire if production
reaches 60,OnO tons of copper content p.a. provided that the
Government guarantees an electricity supply of 254 GWH;
(d)  SODIIIZA is exemnt from all taxes for 5 years after Droduc-
tion start-up;hat it has to pav 25%' of the current export taxes
during the 6th to 10th years, 50% during the 11th and 15th
years, 757 during the 16th to 20th year.and 100%O thereafter.
1/   Sumitano Metal Mining Co. Ltd., Toho Zinc Co. Ltd., Purukawa Mining Co.
Ltd., M'itsui Mining and Smelting Co., Ltd. and NTissho-Iwai Co. Ltd.



ANNEX 3-1
Page 4
13.       SODIM,.IZA's management consists almost entirely of Japanese expatri-
ates and more than 20% of the Company's workers are also Japanese; however,
under its convention with the Zairian Government SODIMIZA is required to hire
and train asmmany Zairian nationals as feasible.
3.   SMTF
14.       SMTF (Societe Miniere de Tenke-Fungurume), a production company
and its affiliate SIPZ (Societe Internationale des MTines du Zaire) dn ex-
ploration Firm, signed in September 1970 a convention with the Government of Zaire
granting mining and exploration rights for 35 years in the Tenke-Fungurume
area along the KDL railway line, north of the GECA14INES concession. SIMZ's
initial exploration concession consisted of 30,692 sq. km. More than two-
thirds have been returned to the Government of Zaire. The resulting final
area under SMTF-SIMZ concession will amount to about 5,000 sq. km. The
SIMZ concession for exploration will expire in five years but is renewable.
Ore reserves of 50 million tons have been proven in the Tenke-Fungurume area,
and an equal amount of reserves are inferred. The deposits are said to be
the richest unexploited ore reserves in Zaire averaging 6.2% copper and 0.4%
cobalt.
15.       SITF-SIMZ is a joint venture between the Government of Zaire (20%
of share capital) and a multi-national consortium (80%) consisting of the US
AMOCO Minerals Company (28%), Charter Consolidated Limited of London (28%),
Mitsui and Company (14%), Onnium Mines, a French Company (7%), and Leon
Templesman and Sons (3%). Charter Consolidated has management contracts for
the two companies -- SMTF and SIM7. Nevertheless, SMTF is required to employ
Zairian nationals on a priority basis.
16.       The Mdining Agreement between the Government of Zaire and SMTF
stipulates that:
(a) the Government of Zaire can purchase 20% of mine production
at a fair ex-plant price;
(b)a all mined ore will be refined in Zaire after a reasonable
period of concentrate production;
(c) a global tax exemption for 5 years after production start-
up followed by a 50% profit tax exemption for 20 years of
production.
17.       STTF is presently constructing the social and industrial infra-
structure at the plant site. Project management of the open pit operation
and metallurgical facilities have been contracted to Fluor Utah, a US
Company.  Although planning and construction activities are under uay, the
date of production start-up depends on the completion of the Inga-Shaba
line. SMTF will receive its entire power supply through the Inga-Shaba
liine which will be complete in late 1977.  S?TF expects to produce
80,000 tons of refined copper in 1978 and reach full capacity (125,000 tons)
in 1980.

paras. 6-17 above indicate substantial increases in Zaire's copper output in
the next 6 years. Table 1 below summarizes the projected copper production
levels until 1980. Conper production equals copper export since domestic

export earnings will not rise parallel with the expansion, but increase only
by about 2-47 p.a. in real terms.
19.       The production projections can be considered realistic.  Neverthee-
less, slower growth is possible if the start-up of S1MTF will be delayed by 6-12
months due to the lack of power through the Inga-Shaba line. In addition,
SODIMIZA may decide to forfeit the exploitation of the Tshinsenda mine.
5.   Long-Term Prospects
20.       After the comnletion of the second 5-Year Develonment Plan (1974-79)
GECAHINES olans to implement a further investment program which would increase
the Company's copper production to 650,000 tons of refined copper by 1984.
This objective will involve the imDrovement of the existing facilities at
Lubumbashi, Luilu and Shituru at an estimated investment cost of Z 170 million
(in 1974 prices).



ANNEX 3-1
Page 6
21.       Future expansion and investment plans of SMTF depend on (i) the
difficulties the Company will encounter during the start-up period (1978-80)
and (ii) future copper and cobalt market prospects. Theoretically, SMTF
could expand its output to 250,000 tons of refined copper at an investment
of 9F 150 million (at 1974 prices).
22.       In addition to the expansion investments outlined above, the 3 copper
mining companies will have to invest a total of more than 9 50 million p.a.
(in 1974 prices) in mine development and replacement to maintain their produc-
tion capacity and productivity.
C.   STRUCTURE OF THE INDUSTRY
1.   Market Share and Comnetitive Position
23.       The production projections above indicate that GECAMIMES will
continue to dominate Zaire's copper industry with a market share of at
least 70%. Nevertheless, after SMTF and SODDflZA will be in full production
Zairian copper producers will compete against each other only to a limited
extenlt. However, copper export coordination is likely, since: (i) Zaire is
member of CIPEC (ii) the new marketing organization for GECAJ-fINES' mineral
products may in the long run market all of Zaire's copper export and (iii)
only GECAMINES and SITTF will compete in the same market segment, i.e.,
primarily in Europe, while SODIMIZA can rely on a captive market through
its long-term supply contracts with its Japanese sponsors.
2.   Integration
24.       Historically the Zairian copper industry has had a high degree of
vertical and horizontal integration. The major reason for this structure is
the location of the Zairian copper industrv in the center of Africa, and the
fact that for decades GECAMINES was the only copper producer. Horizontal
integration develoned and made GECAMINES a relatively self-sufficient producer
with extensive servicing facilities, supply networks, etc.  The existing
structure has forced SODIMIZA as well as SITF to also create their own support
industries.  No snin-off industry from GECAINTES' operation which could pro-
vide services and manpower developed in the nast.
25.       Regarding vertical integration, GWCAMINES and the other conner
producers will be equally concentrated. This development has been increased
by the recent addition of four of IJnion Miniere's ex-subsidiaries in GECAMI'TS.
Consequently, the conner industry included the mining of thie rwry materials
to the nroduction of intermediate, end and by-products. Before independence
forward linlcages, i.e. refining and casting of GECAJMNES' nroducts had been
the prerogative of S0G  and their subsidiaries in Belgium.  However, the in-
dustry is developing strong vertical integration patterned as detailed
in paras. 26 and 27 below.



ATNEX 3-1
Page 7
26.       Refining.  President Mobutu announced in his speech on November
30, 1973 that Zaire's total copper output should be refined domestically by
1980. The existing copper mining companies have been taking steps to reach
this target:
(a)  In 1973 GECAMINES refined about 230,000 tons of its copper
production domestically. The GECAMINES Expansion Project
and investment plans for 1979-84 include refining facilities
for the additional ore output and the present cathode and
blister production. However, Zaire guaranteed SGM until 1980
a supply of 150,000 tons of blister copper and decreasing by
not more than 50,000 tons p.a. thereafter (Annex 3-4, para. 3).
(b) SMTF's total copper output will be refined in Zaire after
a reasonable start-up period.
(c)  According to the 1969 Mining Agreement with the Government
of Zaire, SODITIZA is obliged to construct refining facilities
in Zaire for annual mine output in excess of 60,000 tons of
copper content.
Taking into account the agreements signed by SODIMIZA and GECA,NTES, at
least 90% of Zaire's copper production will be refined locally after 1980.
Zaire's present refining capacity is in line with that of the other CIPEC
countries.
27.       Casting of refined copper to wire bars, cakes, billets, rods, or
special formats represents a further step in copper processing. According to
the agreement dated April 10, 1974 between the Government of Zaire, SCM,
SON's subsidiary Metallurgie Eloboken, SGM will provide technical assistance
to construct and operate a copner casting plant at Maluku. The company
which is planned as a wholly Zairian-o'-med venture, would transform GECAYT\ES'
copper cathodes and produce (i) by 1978, 40,000 tons of cake and 60,000
tons of billets, and (ii) by 1979, an additional 100,000 tons of copDer
rod. Investment costs for the first phase of 100,000 tons of conper have
been estimated at F 40 million.  The plant is expected to create employment
for 380 people of whom not more than 40 would be expatriates.
D.   CONTRIBUTIO1 TO THE ECONOMY
1.   Domestic Value Added and Linkages
28.       No estimate is available of domestic value added in copner per se
though it is likelv that it represents some 80-90 percent of value added in
the mining sector as a whole. The national accounts put this latter amount
at about 9 112 million in constant 1970 Zaires, on average, during 1968-73,
which is on the order of only 13 percent of GDP at factor cost for the period.
However, these estimates, following standard national accounting practice



ANNEX 3-1
Page 8
with respect to indirect taxes, exclude export duties (though not the turn-
over tax), paid the Government, which on GECAMINES' output alone averaged
about g 70 million (in current prices) during 1968-73. Since the price of
copper is determined in international markets, and since the Government has
the options of taking its revenues in the form either of profits tax or --
as sole owner of the company -- in profits per se -- these revenues could be
considered part of the mining industry's contribution to GDP. This adjustment
alone would raise its share to around 20 percent of GDP at factor cost.
29.       The contribution of the copper industry to Zaire's domestic economy
would be substantially higher if it included value added attributable to
activities directly and indirectly linked to the copper industry. Concerning
the former, the most important directly related activities (apart mining and
processing facilities, value added of which is presumably already included in
"mining and minerals processing") are the economic and social services provided
by the industry and included in its cost of production. These consist mainly
of expenditures on housing, education, and health which offer services which
would otherwise be provided by the housing industry or the Government.
30.       In this connection, GECAMINES affords a high standard of social
facilities. The company maintains workers' settlements -- so-called "cites"
-- near its mines and plants which offer housing, primary schools and other
community services such as sports grounds, churches, administrative buildings,
market places and meeting halls. (See Annex 2-7) GECkMINES owned about
21,000 housing units of 2-4 rooms each at twelve sites. Another 2,330 houses
are planned as part of the 1974-79 expansion program. The Company also main-
tains 41 primary schools, 7 secondary schools, a technical secondary school
and 6 schools for basic home economics. Further, the Company provides medical
services free to all workers, emDloys a medical staff of 175 including 40
doctors, and operates hospitals and other facilities with nearly 1,800 beds.
Finally, GECAMINES is responsible for developing and operating its own water
supply and operates its own telephone system.
31.       To the extent that value added has been estimated independently in
sectors directly supplying but not integrated with the copper industry, it
also could be considered part of the copper sector's contribution to the
economy. The most obvious cases concern incomes generated in electricity
production and transnortation. With respect to electric power, the conper
industry now consumes about three quarters of the electricity produced in
the country (see Power Sector Survey). Assuming value added in electricity
and gas are proportional to production, this would add some Z 5-6 million to
the copper industry's annual contribution to domestic income. In connection
with transportation, GECAMINES alone shipped 240,000 tons via Matadi -- the
only all Zairian route -- in 1973. This represents 85 percent of the
annual tonnage shipned over that line.
32.       In connection with trends in value added and its relation to
investment and the labor force, the picture is complicated by the volatility
of copper prices. Nevertheless, over the period 1969-73, average gross



ANNEX 3-1
Page 9
fixed capital of Z 425 million in current prices, generated average annual
value added, in current prices, unadjusted, of Z 120-150 million, yielding
a gross capital output ratio of 3.5-2.8. The ICOR 1/ during the same period,
valuing additional copper output at the average price per ton for the five
years was 2,9.
2.   Employment, Wages and Salaries
33.       Although national accounting methodology may not fully account for
the contribution of the copper sector to the economy, it remains true that
the amount of capital invested in the industry is high in relation to direct
employment and wage incomes. Table 1, Annex 2-7 shows the evolution of
GECAMINES personnel from 1969-1973, which increased from 24,835 to 29,012,
a rise of 17 percent. During this same period copper production increased
by 28 percent, while gross fixed assets rose by about 20 percent. Gross
fixed capital per worker at the end of the period was about Z 15,000, and
new investment per job during the period was about Z 18,500.
34.       Traditionally, GECAMINES has recruited and trained workers from
Zaire, Rwanda and other neighboring countries. According to Zairian legis-
lation not more than 10 percent of workers are to be foreigners and GECAMINES
has continuously decreased its share of foreign workers to meet the legal
target. (See Annex 2-7, para. 3).
35.       In 1973, average remuneration to surface and underground labor was
Z 44 per month. This is increased to Z 77 per month when additions are made
for the cost of family allowances, communal services, retirement and other
charges. Average salaries and charges for expatriate staff are on the order
of Z 1,800 per month and for African staff on the order of Z 400. These rates
imply total labor and staff costs of about Z 60.5 million in 1973, though
remunerations per se were probably about Z 50 million. More than half of this
is accounted for by expatriate salaries and benefits.

product, the analysis comprises not only copper but its co-products -- mainly
cobalt, and zinc as well.

its co-products varied in he range of $446 to $823 million. Although the
volume of exports climbed by about 100,000 tons, the amplitude of the fluctua-
tions is the result largely of variations in the copper price, as can be

seen clearly by comparing 1972, when the average annual copper price on the
London Metal Exchange was at a low for the period of 48 cents per pound, with
1973, when it averaged 81 cents per pound. On the average for the five year
period, GECAMINES' exports were valued at US$538 million and accounted for
68 percent of the total value of merchandise exports and for 56 percent of
total current foreign exchange earnings.
38.       Net foreign exchange earnings were, of course, less though they
were still substantial. Thus, on average, GECAMINES paid the equivalent of
some US$100 million per year for imports of intermediate and capital goods,
US$22 million per year in expatriate salaries, and US$30 million per year
in financial changes and compensation payments. Average annual net foreign
exchange earnings were thus on the order of US$372 million, of which about
US$250 million per year equivalent was paid to the Government in taxes.
39.       Two observations are in order with respect to the net foreign
exchange earnings of GECAMINES. First, in the medium term, foreign exchange
expenditures are not compressible. Hence, when copper prices change,
fluctuations in net foreign exchange earnings are higher percentage-wise
than in gross revenues. This increases the vulnerability of the economy
to price changes. Second, about 15% of foreign exchange expenditure is
salary payments to expatriates. This confirms the view that over the long
term, there is room for savings through the training and placement of
Zairian in staff positions.
4.   Public Revenue Effect
40.       The system of taxation of copper and its co-products embraces
taxes and duties on exports, imports, wages and salaries, profits and mis-
cellaneous taxes and fees. Export taxes generate the largest part of the
public mining revenue and comprise four types: (i) the export duty per se,
which is levied as a flat rate on a set tax reference price, or "base value,"
(40 percent in the case of copper); (ii) a statistical tax, levied on the same
base (1% for copper); (iii) since 1969, a supplementary export surcharge
levied on base values in excess of predetermined minimum (at present, the
base value of electrolytic copper above which the surcharge is imposed is
Z 340 per ton; this value is different for other types of copper); and
(iv) a turnover tax levied on c.i.f. value (7 percent for both copper and
cobalt). Profits are taxed at 40 percent and wages and salaries at differ-
ential rates.
41.       In the context of the Investment Code and in order to stimulate an
expansion of mining, the Government has also passed conventions or ordonances
exempting earnings generated by new investment from various taxes. In the
case of GECAMINES revenues earned on output attributable to its expansion
programs have been exonerated from taxation for 5-year periods. Thus taxable
production for the period 1970-74 was fixed at 360,000 tons, i.e. the company's
capacity prior to the investment program, and is expected to be set at
460,000 tons from 1975-82. As noted above, SODIMIZA is exempt from all



ANNEX 3-1
Page 12
taxes for five years following start-up, and thereafter benefits from a re-
duction in the tax burden through the 20th year. Similarly, SMTF will be
exonerated from taxes for five years, and subject only to 50% of profits tax
thereafter through the 20th year.
42.       The graph on page 15 of this Annex shows the relation between reve-
nues per ton from export duties (not including the turnover tax), the c.i.f.
copper price, the f.o.b. value and the "base value". Table 4 and p.16
explain the derivation. At base values of Z 340 per ton or less, the com-
bined rate of export and statistical tax is 41 percent. This corresponds to
f.o.b. values of Z 480 per ton or less and an effective tax rate of 29.08
percent. Between base values of Z 340-382, the export surcharge enters into
effect and increases by legally specified increments. For base values above,
Z 382 per ton, a marginal rate of 40 percent is applied. This leads to
effective export duty rates on the f.o.b. value of up to 50 percent, as the
copper price increases. Base values are set each month on the basis of world
market Prices prevailing three months earlier. This lag means that monthly
revenues are lower than they would be on the basis of current price quota-
tions, wqhen prices are rising, and higher than they would be on the basis
of current price quotations, when prices are falling.
43.       The average value of total taxes paid by GECAMIINES during 1969-73
was Z 107.4 million, contributing 45 percent of Zaire's total tax revenues.
In this period, they fell to a low of Z 93.4 million in 1971 and reached a
high of Z 173.7 in 1973 -- an increase of almost 86 percent -- in resnonse
mainly to copper price increases. The average burden on company sales f.o.b.
of all tax payments was 49 Percent. It is to be observed, however, that
this burden declined to less than 40 percent in 1973, principally because of
the exoneration from all taxes above an annual production of 360,000 tons of
copper and 11,000 tons of cobalt. Table 3 summarizes direct and indirect
taxes paid or generated by GECAMINES.

3.   Value c.i.f. x .0907 - value c.i.f. (or market value) in
U.S. cents per lb.
- IZ = 200 U.S. cents
- 1 Metric Ton - 2,204.6 lbs.
- 200 - 2,204.6 = .0907
4.   Revenues from export tax plus statistical tax - 41% of "Base" value
Supplementary export tax (D.S.C.)

value of copper products is greater than:

5.   Economic Effects of Dependence on Copper
44.       As described above, the copper industry is the largest single source
of Zaire's public revenues and foreign exchange. These benefits have not, of
course, been without their costs. Most obvious is the fact that the financial
situation of the economv is particularly vulnerable to fluctuations in the
copper price. Less obvious but no less important is the impact of the industry
on domestic price and cost relations, which may no longer reflect the scarcity
value of productive factors and final goods and services to the economy. In
particular, the availability of government revenues and the relatively high
standard of living in the enclave economy are both factors contributing to
higher wages and prices in other sectors of the economy, leading to reductions
in the competitiveness of industrial and agricultural products in international
markets. While it might be hoped that incomes accruing to the administration
and the industry on which it depends would generate higher demand for and
increased supplies of local agricultural and processed goods, there is little
evidence that this has happened in Zaire. On the contrary, so far the level of
incomes appears to have served largely to increase imports of food and other
consumer goods.    -            - -
Industrial Projects Department
October 1974



ANNEX 3-2
Page 1
ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
THE COPPER MARKET
1.        Copper is one of the most important commodities traded in the world.
The value of annual copper consumption exceeds that of all other metals with
the exception of steel, representing almost US$20 billion in 1973. Copper
can also be characterized by another feature, its price instability. Low
short-term elasticities of demand and supply generate huge and volatile
changes in prices when imbalances of supply and demand occur, and thus create
problems for the consumers and, very importantly, the copper exporting LDC's.
A. WORLD COPPER RESERVES
2.        Copper is found in association with other metals, such as nickel
and platinum in ultrabasic intrusives; zinc, and cadmium in volcanic-
sedimentary deposits; Molybdenum in hydrothermal deposits; and cobalt in
sedimentary deposits. All copper ores contain small quantities of gold and
silver. The copper content of the ore varies widely from an average of
0.3-2% for intrusive deposits, 0.5-3.0% for replacement deposits and 0.5-5%
for sedimentary deposits.
3.        Geographically, copper deposits are found all over the world; however,
four major copper areas can be distinguished:
1)   The African copper belt, with deposits of up to 6.5% copper
in Zambia and Zaire. These contain about 25% of the known
copper reserves in the Western world;
2)   Eastern Canada, accounting for about 57 of world copper
reserves with deposits containing 0.5-2.0% copper,
3)   The Western Hemisphere Mountain Chain including deposits
in Alaska, British Columbia, western USA, Mexico, Peru,
Bolivia and Chile which account for nearly 60% of copper
reserves in the world (ex-CPE); 1/ and
This belt continues around the Pacific with deposits on
4)   The East Asian Coast and island chain, particularly, the
Philippines, Australia, Bougainville, Mainland China, etc.
Smaller but still significant reserves totalling about 20% of world
reserves are found in U.S.S.R., Yugoslavia, Poland, Finland, Scandinavia,
Turkey, Romania, Cyprus, Spain, Portugal, India and several other countries.
1/   In this Annex "world" refers to all countries but the "centrally
planned economics".



ANNEX 3-2
Page 2
4.        Published reserves have increased over the past years (as illustrated
by the table below), while the average copper content of reserves have declined
from an average of 2.5% in 1966 to an estimated 1.1% in 1972.

The notable increase in published reserves is based on the discovery
of new deposits as well as the inclusions of already known copper deposits whose
exploitation became economically viable with more advanced mining and meta.. lur-
gical technology. Clearly, published proven reserves are generally conservatil..
estimates and there is no doubt that probable reserves represent several times
the recoverable copper recorded as proven reserves.  In the future, substantia:
additional reserves are likely to be discovered in the African, East-Asian,
East-European-Turkish copper belts, as well as in deep sea nodules. If mirie
production were to continue at the present rate of exploitation, (7 millioni
tons ner year) published proven reserves would be exhausted by the year 2014.
5.        Copper reserves have not only increased with time, but they vary
with copper price level, i.e., reserves depend on the financial return in
copper exploitation. According to the US Bureau of Mines, in 1974, World
copper reserves range from 304 million tons to 418 million tons for prices
of 69 cents/lb. to 102 cents/lb. respectively, as illustrated below.

B. COPPER SUPPLY AND DEMAND
A market flow chart of copper is given as Chart 2 at the end of
this Annex.
1.   World Copper Supply
6.        The world's copper supply comes from two main sources, either (i)
from primary or mine production of (ii) from secondary or scrap recovery
and is used in two different forms, i.e., either as (i) refined copper from
primary or old or new scrap; or as (ii) scrap for direct use. This inter-
relationship complicates past and projected copper supply statistics and can
be illustrated as follows:



ANNEX 3-2
Page 4
Chart 1: Copper Supply Pattern
Old Scrap
Cnston re
U  Mine-           Ref 3Oing           o f Reined   Od Serap   Use of           Sc i
Productio:nj      p  'A                Coj22er   ,Scra
New Scrap                 New Scrap
New scrap is generated by the fabrication process and recovered at
the manufacturers plant or refinery. Both old and new scrap are recycled as
refined copper or scrap for direct use by consumers. Most of the scrap
recovered as copper alloy is used directly whereas pure copper scrap is
refined a second time.
Primary or Copper Mine Production
7.        Table 1 on page 5 of this Annex presents the world mine production
of copper (19-O-73) and Table 2 on page 6 indicates the shares of major copper
producing countries. Copper mine production in the Western World increased
by an average of 3.7% p.a. during the sixties and by 4.3% p.a. during the
1970-73 period. The 5 major copper mining countries, accounting for about
70% of world output (ex CPE), are the USA, Canada, Chile, Zambia and Zaire.
Over the years Zaire has about maintained its share in the world market,
ranking 5th among copper mining countries. In 1973, 36% of the world's
primary copper production was mined in CIPEC-countries (for details on CIPEC
see paras 26-28) compared to 45.1% in 1959. The decreasing production share
results from the relative low growth rates of the Chilean and Zambian mines
(2.0% p.a. and 1.8%p.a.) during this period.

8.        Primary copper production is basically a function of available
productive capacity and demand for copper. In recent years actual primary
production often fell short of primary production capacity because of strikes
and other exogenous factors. Copper prices have some short-term influence on
production as copper producers try to increase output at existing mines during
high price periods. However, flexibility of output is not significant. Very
low copper prices influenced primary production in 1960-63: producers cut
down production to maintain a floor price of about 29 cents.
9.        Copper prices influence investment decisions and, therefore, have a
long--term impact on primary production. But in the last decade political fac-
tors were partly responsible for the timing of investment:  Chile, Peru, and
African countries had to change or clearly define their copper policies before
copper companies were ready to undertake expansion programs. High world copper
prices and feasible projects alone did not provide sufficient incentives. In
general, it takes between three and six years to open up a new copper mine or
to substantially increase capacity of an existing one.

Refined Copper Production
10.       World production of refined copper increased by an average of 4.1%
per year over the 1960-73 period and reached 6.6 million tons in 1973 (see
table 3, page 7). A comparison of the geographical location of the world's
mine production and refinery output (tables 1, .2 and 4) clearly shows that
traditionally refining facilities were located near to consumer markets, such
as Europe, North America and Japan. However, refining facilities in Africa
and South America are being expanded. For example copper mine production in
Africa multiplied by 2.4 from 1952-72 whereas production of refined copper
quadrupled during the same period. The biggest expansion of refinery capacity
occurred, however, in Japan where refined copper output has been increasing by
an average of 11% per annum during the sixties.

Scrap
11.       The supply of copper scrap is rather volatile and fluctuates with
the price of refined copper, recovery cost and the degree of scarcity of cer-
tain copper products on the market. As indicated in table 5 the production
of new and old scrap decreased by 10% from a high of 3.5 million tons in 1969
to 3.1 million tons in 1972. Scrap is primarily used directly by manufac-
turers, so that secondary refined copper amounted to only 24% of total scrap
production.

In the long-run scrap will account on the average for not more than
40% of the world copper production. Scrap has been recovered primarily from
cables, transformers, motors etc. so that with growing substitution of copper
by aluminum the availability of scrap is likely to decline.. However on the
other hand improvements in scrap recovery technologies may make the production
of secondary copper more economical.
Copper Stocks
13.       Copper stocks play an important role as market indicators and
generally reflect respective price movements. At the London Metal Exchange
(LYE) warehouses stocks were of the order of 20,000 tons in mid-June 1974
compared to about 200,000 tons at the beginning of 1973. However, information
on total stocks held by producers, trade and users is difficult to obtain and
its analysis causes some problems. Stocks are also being held by some govern-
ments. The most important government stockpile used to be held in the U.S.
For many years the stockpile target stood at 750,000 short tons but actual
stocks of 250,000-350,000 short tons were held. Recently, a decision was
taken to sell all stockpiled copper. The sales program has now been completed,
without any market disruption as supply was very tight. Stockpile sales were
made according to bids received and selling prices were considerably higher
than the US producers' price.
2.   World Copper Demand
Uses of Copper
14.       Copper's technical qualities, mainly its electric conductivity,
corrosion resistance, thermal conductivity, maleability, ductibility and



ANNEX 3-2
Page 11
easiness of join, make it one of the most important materials of modern
industry. The use of copper and its alloys is universal. They are prac-
tically synonymous with all things electrical and have myriad uses in elec-
trical equipment and supplies, construction, transportation communications,
appliances, utensils, jewelry and other applications.
The breakdown of copper consumption in different industrial sectors
is shown below. It does vary only slightly between countries and no marked
differences exist between consumption patterns in developed and developing
countries.
Table 7:  Copper Consumption by Sector
Sector                       USA /1          West Germany /2            UK /2
(%)              . (%)                    (
Electrical                  52.0                  56.8                 43.0
Construction                 18.0                 15.4                 24.0
Machinery                    12.4                 14.5                 12.5
Transportation                9.4                  9.2                 14.5
Other                         8.2                  4.1                  6.0
/1   1972 industrial survey.
/2   1967 figures.
The largest use of copper is in electrical equipment and supplies,
communications, including electronics, telephone and telegraph wire and cable.
The manufacture of electric motors, power generator sets, dynamotors, fans,
industrial controls, power distribution systems and electrical instruments
require large quantities of copper for the best electrical performance. While
aluminum is used for virtually all high-voltage overhead power transmission
lines, copper presently dominates in underground line and small wire markets,
since copper is corrosion resistant.
15.       The noncorrosive properties of copper and its alloys result in many
uses in building construction for roofing, plumbing, builders' hardware and
functional decorative applications. Copper finds also widespread application
in the production of many types of nonelectrical industrial machinery, such as
heat exchangers, turbines, oil baffles, check valves, plates, sheets, bars
and copper tubing. About 10% of the copper consumed in the world is used in
transportation including motor vehicles, railroads, and marine uses. This
results both from the trend toward greater convenience and comfort such as
power windows, seats, brakes, stearing and airconditioning, but also from the
more utilitarian uses in radiators, heaters, defrosters, oil lines, etc.
Substitution
16.       While the properties of copper are almost irreplaceable in some
applicatons it faces competition in other areas of consumption from aluminum,
plastics, steel, glass, and other materials. In many uses requiring conduction



ANNEX 3-2
Page 12
of heat or electrical energy, aluminum has displaced copper. There has been
an accelerated shift to the use of steel for brass in shell cases and printed
electrical circuits have come into the field as substitute of copper wire.
17.       Aluminum and stainless steels have also reduced the use of copper
in the building industry. Table 12 below shows the relative consumption in-
crease during the sixties in West Germany and Japan.
Table 8: Copper and Aluminum Consumption
Year                          Copper  _                 Aluminum
(tons)         (%)          (tons)        (%)
1961                 716,570         94.9          38,620         5.1

Technology has furthered substitution for copper by the introduction of the
numerous cladmetals, including copper clad which use proportionally less
copper per unit. Plastic tubing substitutes for some copper pipe in many
applications in building and the automotive industry.
18.       The reasons for substitution are not only periods of short supply
of copper resulting in high price levels but also sustained price fluctuations
which creates uncertainty among manufacturers. Reverse substitution is rela-
tively difficult. Substitution for an end-user involves extensive retooling
and planning so that reverse substitution is only likely to occur if there
are unforeseen problems with substitute materials.
19.       Substitution has not been clearly felt because copper consumption
has been growing in absolute terms. This has been mainly due to the over-
proportional growth of the electrical and transport sectors where copper has
an established position. As this dynamism is likely to continue, there should
be scope for both further substitution and steady growth of copper consumption.
The impact of higher energy cost on aluminum and plastic prices cannot yet be
assessed. On the one hand if copper prices increase less rapidly than prices
of its substitute materials, the substitution trend may halt or even reverse.
On the other hand new production processes, for aluminum, for example could
reduce costs substantially in the next 5-10 years, while no great technological
breakthrough is expected in the copper industry.



ANNEX 3-2
Page 13

21.       Consumption of refined copper is heavily concentrated in the devel-
oped countries. In 1973, the USA, Canada, Western Europe and Japan accounted
for nearly 92% of western world consumption of refined copper. Growth of re-
fined copper consumption is closely correlated with growth of industrial pro-
duction 1/ and averaged 4.4% for the developed countries during the 1952-73
period. Although the long-term growth pattern has been steady during the
sixties and seventies regional growth rates varied substantially as indicated
below:

22.       Copper consumption increased substantially in developing countries
with the beginning of industrialization. In Brazil copper consumption grew
about 60% more rapidly than other economic indicators--a phenomenon which
had been observed in Japan during the sixties. Copper consumption in Japan
is likely to stabilize during the coming decade and to correlate more closely
with industrial production growth as in Europe and the USA. The portion of
Europe's refined copper consumption in the Western World which reached 50%
in 1958, appears to stabilize now at about 40%.
3.   International Trade
23.       Almost all international transactions of copper are made in primary
copper. Exports of scrap occurs relatively seldom, and are limited nearly ex-
clusively to US exports. International trade of copper as represented by net
1/   On average the copper growth rate has been equivalent to 80% of the
industrial production growth rate. However, copper growth rates may
exceed industrial growth in rapidly expanding economies.

export countries.

During the seventies the major increase of exports occurred in ores
and concentrates from developed countries, such as Canada and the USA and non-
CIPEC developing countries such as Papua-New Guinea and the Philippines. Net
exports of ore and concentrates grew at an average 24%.
25.       It is debatable if the trend of shipping concentrates will continue.
On the one hand, the copper consuming countries Japan, Europe, and the U.S.
have expanded their smelter and refining capacities over the past years and
are anxious to sign long-term delivery contracts for concentrates.  The cheaper
and more efficient handling and transport techniques have made it possible to
shift smrlting and refining of concentrates from the mine sites to the consumer
countries' ports. On the other hand, the copper exporting countries will try
to maximize export earnings and domestic value added and force the mining com-
panies to expand smelting and refining on site. In addition, strict pollution
control in developed countries particularly the USA and Canada may favor treat-
ment of the copper ore in developing countries, although smelters have been
able to operate competitively in Europe for many years under pollution regula-
tions that have just been adopted in the US.
CIPEC
26.       CIPEC (Conseil intergouvernemental des pays exportateurs de cuivre)
was formed in 1967 by Zambia, Chile, Zaire and Peru. In 1972, these four
countries accounted for 37.5% of the world's copper mine production, 20.6% of
refined copper production and 57% of copper exports, as detailed in Table 15
below:
Table 15: Copper Production and Exports of CIPEC Countries (1972)
(000 tons)

27.       The objectives of this organization are the development of the copper
industry in these countries, the maximization and stabilization of copper export
earnings and the manufacture of copper products in CIPEC countries. In the
past CIPEC has not been very successful in achieving these goals.  There have
been recurrent statements by CIPEC member countries and expectations in the



ANNEX 3-2
Page 20
copper trade that CIPEC would try to "stabilize" the copper price or at least
introduce a floor price. It is argued that cooperation between CIPEC and major
integrated copper producers in industrialized countries could produce an OPEC-
type action with good chances for success. However, copper producing countries
are not in as strong a position as the OPEC countries: first; substitution of
copper by other metals would be a major reason for limited price increases;
second; the foreign exchange reserves of the CIPEC countries are relatively
low, so that the risks of taking sustained actions will be considerable. In
view of widely different interests between CIPEC members and copper companies
in the developed countries, the formation of a copper cartel or coordinated
oligopolistic action of copper producers is unlikely. The recent announcement
by CIPEC members of a 10% cut in shipments represented unified action on their
part for the first time. In view of the statistical surplus in supply, this
decision must be regarded as good business sense. Many U.S. and Canadian
producers have also announced production cutbacks and more producers are likely
to follow. The current statistical surplus is estimated at an annual rate of
400,000 tons. It seems likely that the cutbacks would preclude further declines
in prices, and also that the cycle time of low prices will be shortened.
28.       It is noteworthy in this context that production costs in CIPEC
countries show substantial differences. At the same time all CIPEC members
recently announced major expansion plans and some new projects are already
being implemented. Many of the new projects are being developed in cooperation
with copper companies based in developed countries. It does not appear that
there is any coordination of expansion plans. This seems to support the con-
clusion that CIPEC chances for drastic price action are rather dim.
4.   Supply/Demand Prospects
World Supply of Primary Copper
29.       A projection of world copper mine capacity is subject to a more
than usual uncertainty at this time. Based on estimates made by industry
sources and by Bank staff, world mine capacity in 1980 has been projected by
region and by country as presented in Table 16. "Low", "High" and "Most likely"
estimates are shown for major countries and regions for 1980. World mine capa-
city is projected to increase from 6.6 million tons at the beginning of 1974
to 8.1-9.6 million tons by 1980. No detailed projection by country or region
is possible for 1985.
30.       Latin American capacity depends on developments in Chile in the
coming 6 years; mine capacity could increase to 1.325 million tons or could
increase only moderately to 1.1 million tons by 1980. Chile presents the
largest uncertain factor on the supply side of the world copper market for the
next several years. Beyond 1980, possible developments in Panama present addi-
tional uncertainty due to the possible development of a large copper mine proj-
ect, the Cerro Colorado. However, for the present it seems unlikely that this
mine will be in operation by 1980. Other uncertainties in this region exist
in Peru and Mexico.

31.       Among the developed countries, significant expansions are possible
in the United States, Canada, Australia and South Africa. However, recent
trends in Canada and Australia towards reorientation of their mineral invest-
ment policies could have serious dampening effects on the pace of investments
in copper mining capacity in these countries.
32.       One of the problems for a rapid expansion of the world copper mine
capacity is the availability of capital. Capital investment required to estab-
lish a capacity to produce refined copper is estimated at around US$4,000
(in 1974 constant terms) for each annual ton of capacity. 1/ Thus, if the
"high" estimate of projected capacity, i.e., 9.5 million tons per annum, is
to be realized by 1980, $12 billion (in 1974 constant terms) would be required
to build the additional 3.0 million-ton capacity needed.
33.       Table 17 indicates the implications of various possible magnitudes
of annual investment in the world copper industry for (i) additional capacity
to be created by 1980, (ii) total capacity available at the beginning of 1980
and (iii) mine production in 1980. Between the end of 1968 and the end of
1973, world mine capacity increased from 4.98 million tons to 6.61 million
tons, by 0.41 million tons per annum, or at 7.2% per annum.

for Copper", American Metal Market Forum, London 1971). Allowing for the
inflation that has occurred since 1971 and for estimated extra costs
needed for pollution controls in smelting and refining, a rule-of-thumb
estimate of US$4,000 per ton of capacity has been arrived at. This may
be an overestimate to the extent that new capacity results from an ex-
pansion of already existing capacity.



ANNEX 3-2
Page 23
Supply/Demand Balance
34.       At this point in time copper demand trojections are extremely
difficult. On the one hand, a continuing economic slow down in the developed
countries may limit copper consumption growth, while on the other hand, copper
may also benefit from the relatively more rapid increase in the prices of alu-
minium and petrochemicals. Although it will probably not regain markets pre-
viously lost to aluminum, copper's competitive Dosition vis-a-vis aluminum
and plastic have now improved as a result of sharp increases in petroleum
prices.
For the purpose of this report two hypotheses for future refined copper con-
sumptions will be analyzed:
a)   Hypothesis I assumes that while economic growth in developed
countries and non-oil-exporting developing countries might
somewhat slow down in the coming decade, industrialization in
oil-exporting countries would accelerate in the future and
therefore consumDtion of refined copoer will grow at 4.5% per
annum in line with the long-term growth trend of consumption
over the 1970-73 period;
b)   Hypothesis II assumes that world consumption of refined copper
will increase by 3.2% per annum until 1977 and 4.0% per annum,
thereafter in view of the present economic stagnation and
inflation. Hypothesis II represents the most likely demand
forecast.
35.       As presented in table 18, world refined copper consumption would
increase by 1980 to 9.2 million tons under hypothesis I and 8.8 million tons
under hypothesis II. In contrast, projected refined copper supply for the
Western World is estimated at 9.3 million tons in 1980. During the seventies
there is a slight possibility of an oversupply of refined copper ranging from
100,000 tons per year to 700,000 tons depending on the accuracy of projected
consumption growth rate. An additional reservation must be made: The supply/
demand balance assumes that over the coming 5 years the supply of secondary
refined copper will remain at about 15% of total refined copper production.
However, since the supply of secondary copper has been extremely price
elastic, it may well be that with falling prices during an oversupply situa-
tion the supply of secondary refined copper would drop, and thus exact a new
market equilibrium.
C. PRICES
1.   Pricing System
36.       Copper is priced in three main markets, the London Metal Exchange
(LME) the Commodity Exchange in New York (COMEX) and the producer controlled
markets.

ANNEX 3-2
Page 25
LME
37.       The London Metal Exchange is the most important "pricing" market
for copper. Less than 10% of copper sales are actually delivered from LME
stocks, but the bulk of copper's international trade in refined copper is
undertaken directly by producers and consumers at prices prevailing at the
LME around the date of shipment. Metal dealers also quote LME prices for
their transactions. The LME operates on both cash and future contracts
which allows hedging operations against inventory losses and currency realign-
ments. During 1973 and 1974 there has been an increase in non-industry specu-
lations in copper to hedge against inflation and exchange rate changes.
38.       The LME is European biased since it reflects mainly the short term
supply-demand outlook in Europe, where its warehouses are located (UK, Germany,
Holland and Belgium). Due to its physical thinness and marginal character
speculation exaggerates ups and downs on a very short time basis, so that
daily up and downs of more than 5% are not uncommon.
39.       Usually, sales contracts for refined copper are signed on an annual
basis and specify twelve monthly quotas to be shipped by the seller. Prices
are related to the LME's settlement price (wirebars' moving seller cash prices)
on a cif basis. The typical buyer can price his quotas during a 2 month period
(covering the shipment date) the prices of any particular day and communicate
it to the seller not later than noon of the following day. Certain restric-
tions to this pricing mechanism do exist, e.g., not more than 25% of a
monthly quota can be priced in one day and not more than 50% in one week.
On the average the buyers can gain a fraction of a cent per pound by carefully
choosing the pricing days. Copper concentrates are bought on long-term con-
tracts which can be as long as 10-15 years, but will generally be at the order
of 3-5 years. Pricing is usually based on the monthly average of the LME cash
wirebar quotations/minus treatment costs.
COMEX
40.       The Commodity Exchange in New York is smaller and far less signifi-
cant than the LME, but trades cash and future contracts of up to 14 months.
Prices are influenced by the U.S. producer price. Due to arbitration, COMEX
quotes differ usually not more than a few cents/lb. from the LME price quotation.
Producer Prices
41.       The U.S., Canada and the COMECON  countries trade copper on the basis
of producer prices, a selling price fixed by the producers which is generally
kept unchanged for several months. Chile and Zambia quoted producer prices
until 1967 when the CIPEC countries decided to trade according to LME price
quotations. The following table compares producers and LME prices during the
1960-73 period.

42.       Producer price changes follow mainly operating cost increases.
In the past average producer prices have been lower than the LME quotations,
so that U.S. consumers rely on imports only to satisfy their marginal needs.
In general, consumers of producer markets do not switch to imports when the
LME price drops significantly, since refined copper is sold on one year con-
tracts and import tariffs may equalize price advantages.
2.   Past Copper Price Development
43.       Copper prices have not fallen appreciably below the 30 cent level
since the end of the Korean war, except briefly in 1957/58. Annual averages
of both U.S. Producer and London Metal Exchange (LME) prices fluctuated with
in a range of 29-32 cents per pound (33.5-37.0 cents per pound at 1968 prices)
form 1953/54 to 1964. They rose about 40 percent above this range (to over
40 cents per pound) in 1955/56, and fell about 15-20 percent below it in
1957/58 (to 25.8 cents per pound in the U.S. and to 24.7 cents per pound on
the IME).



ANNEX 3-2
Page 27
44.       During the 1953-63 period the copper trade generally believed that
major primary producers regarded 30 cents per pound in the U.S. (and a slightly
lower LME quotation) as a target price. In the early 1960's non-US producers
intervened directly in the LME to hold the price above 29 cents at a time when
excess capacity caused all major producers to cut production. In 1965, non-US
producers set a price of 29.5 cents independently of the LME.
45.       Since 1965 copper prices have risen far above the earlier "normal"
level. Even in the U.S. where Government pressure held the domestic copper
price to 36 cents until the end of 1966, it subsequently rose to 61 cents by
April, 1970. Outside the U.S. prices have risen much faster. Since the out-
set of 1965 the LME price has never been less than 45 cents. In March 1966,
when producer pricing was abandoned, LME spot price quotations reached 85 cents
per pound. This was followed by a decline and then another peak at 78 cents
in March, 1968 during the U.S. copper strike. Prices declined in the spring
and summer of 1968 after the strike was settled, but another rise started in
mid-1968, continued over 1969, and in 1970, reached monthly averages of 73 cents
in January; 75 cents in February; and 79 cents in March. The LME average price
for 1970 was 64 cents, only slightly below the 1969 average of 66 cents. How-
ever, prices had started a new decline in April 1970, falling to 47 cents in
December of that year. They remained at about this level throughout 1971,
averaging 49 cents for the year. Industrial production slowed down, copper
stocks increased, and only production difficulties in some producer countries
kept prices above the 45 cent average. This situation did not basically change
in 1972. Stocks continued to increase and the LME price averaged 49 cents for
the year. The dispute between Kennecott and Chile brought an additional element
of uncertainty to the world copper market which helped maintain price levels
despite the increase in stocks. Early in 1973 the problems in Chile, various
strikes at mines and refineries elsewhere and transport problems in Zambia
pushed copper prices upwards. The new monetary crisis leading to a devaluation
of the stocks and substantial increase in the free gold price added to the pres-
sure on copper prices. During January-March 1973 the LME price averaged 59 cents
and daily quotations went above 70 cents in March for the first time since 1970.
Other extraordinary events followed, including the largest number of "forces
majeure" in any year since 1945, the highest rate of international economic
growth in many years, and strong world inflation. All these factors played
a role in pushing LME prices to $1.20/lb. in early December. Price escalation
was encouraged by massive speculation in commodities which accompanied the
monetary and the oil crisis.
46.       The volatility of the world copper market since 1964-72 was due to
a number of factors, the most important of which were: a) uncertainties con-
nected with the 1964 presidential elections in Chile, and with Zambia becoming
independent in 1965; b) a U.S. maritime strike in 1965; c) miners' strikes in
Chile (1965/66), the U.S. (1967/68), and Canada (1969); d) saled from the U.S.
copper stockpile (1966); e) delayed implementation of copper expansion programs;
f) monetary crises leading to devaluations of sterling (1967), and of the
French franc (1968), as well as revaluation of German mark (1969), and new cur-
rency alignments at the end of 1971 and early in 1973; g) copper purchases by
Communist China and the USSR; h) the economic boom in industrial nations in



ANNEX 3-2
Page 28
1969, the general slump in economic activity in 1971/72, and the signs of a new
industrial boom appearing late in 1972; i) the substantial shortfall of Chile's
copper production from previously announced expansion targets; and production
of export problems elsewhere (Zambia, 1971 and 1973; Belgium and Canada, 1973);
and J) the impact of US pollution controls on refinery production (1972/73).
47.       Prices remained high during the first five months of 1974, averaging
about $120/lb. However, in real terms copper prices in 1973 were lower than
in 1969 and 1970. Even back in 1966 copper prices in real terms had been 33
percent higher than in 1973, and the recent price of $1.20/lb. is still slightly
below the record high of 1966 (see table 18). The recent extraordinary price
increase seemed to be influenced mainly by the following factors: first,
uncertainty about the impact of the oil crisis; second, the unknown size of
investment in copper as a hedge against devaluation; third, the increased
demand from China which has been a copper purchaser to a far greater extent
than ever before, though actual figures are not yet available; fourth the
uncertain outlook for a reduction of inflation rates in all industrialized
countries, and finally the possibility of a major strike in the US copper
industry after June 1974 when the 3 year labor contracts were up for renego-
tiation.
48.       Copper prices fell significantly after June 1974 to 56 cents/lb.
in December 1974. Several reasons related to demand decreases have been
quoted for this dramatic price drop; first, the US strike did not materialize
in the expected magnitude so that consumers are diminishing the level of their
stocks; second speculation in copper by non-industry sources has declined to
its normal level; third, the Japanese industry had built up its copper stock
during the first half of 1974 because of a rapid drop in copper consumption,
and finally industrial growth in the developing countries continues to be
slow during the second half of 1974.



ANNEX 3-2
Page 29
3.   Price Projections
49.       Through 1980, production capacity outside the centrally planned
economies (CPE's) is expected to increase from 6.6 million t in 1974 to 8.1
- 9.6 million tons in 1980. If production facilities were to be operated at
93 percent of capacity -- a reasonable assumption in line with past experi-
ence -- the result will be supply surplus by 1980. This assumes: (a) no long
strikes, no political disturbances affecting copper production, etc. beyond
those which have continuously interferred with "normal" production over the
past 15 years; (b) average completion of the various expansion programs; (c)
an increase in net imports by the CPE's from about 100,000 t in recent years
to 200,000 t by 1980. Under these conditions, Zaire and the other copper
exporting countries are expected to encounter no difficulties in marketing
their expanding copper production. The major question is the prices this
production will command.
50.       Traditionally long-term price projections of copper have been based
on either (i) extrapolation of past price trends or (ii) the long-term cost/price
relationship at the equilibrium level. The following paragraphs discuss the
results of both methods.
Price Extrapolations
51.       Despite violent fluctuations copper prices have followed a long-term
upward trend in real terms; in 1973 terms they rose from an average level of
$0.435/lb during the 1929-38 decade to $0.650/lb during the 1949-58 decade and
to $0.740/lb over the 1959-68 decade. The 1969-1973 average was $0.808/lb.
Assuming continuation of the long-term post-war trend, real copper prices in
1973 terms would reach $0.885/lb in 1980 and $0.920/lb in 1985. With inflation
projected at an annual rate of 8.8 percent between 1973 and 1980 and at 6.0 per-
cent for 1980-85 copper prices in current terms would reach $1.60/lb in 1980
and $2.23/lb in 1985. Projections by trend extrapolation depend to a large
extent on the selection of the base period which can alter significantly the
results. For example, if price projections are based on an average price of
$0.74/lb 1/ (in 1973 terms) for the 1969-75 period instead of $0.808/lb during
1969-73, by 1980 copper prices in current terms would reach $1.33/lb instead
of $1.60/lb - a difference of nearly 20%.
Price/Cost Relationships
52.       Data on production costs are difficult to obtain.  One expert quoted
them as averaging $0.130/lb in 1952, $0.241/lb in 1965 and $0.32/lb in 1970,
an average annual nominal increase of 5.2 percent between 1952 and 1970, and
I/   On the basis of actual copper prices in January-October 1974 and short-
term projections for the coming 12 months, average prices of US$0.92/lb
in 1974 and US$0.73/lb in 1975 can be estimated (see paras. 57-58).

Page 31
of 5.8 percent between 1965 and 1970. 1/ Industry sources in developed countries
state that since 1970 the rate of production cost increases has accelerated,
not only because of circumstances of exploitation but also because of special
developments with respect to pollution controls. The extra costs of pollution
controls for existing smelting and refining facilities vary widely depending on
processes used but rough estimates indicate that they fall within the range of
10-15 percent of present unit cost of production. However, cost of pollution
control influence production cost only in countries which have only recently
adopted strict standards, i.e., the USA, Japan, and Canada. Since invest-
ments in pollution control have been phased over a period of time and are
necessary for only some copper producing countries, it is likely that the
real cost increases due to pollution controls will be moderate.
53.       Based on an average unit cost of copper production of $0.32/lb in
1970 prices, cost increases of 2 percent per annum in real terms and an average
inflation rate of 8.8 percent per annum during the seventies, by 1980 copper
production cost will reach US$0.39/lb in 1970 prices and US$0.89/lb in current
prices. However, it must be noted that the above cost projection may over-
estimate actual cost. The unit production cost includes royalties, export and
other taxes, fixed cost items, etc. which may not increase at the same rate
as general world inflation.
54.       Statistically, the market price of copper has had a certain relation-
ship to the average price of competitive copper. 2/. The "Notman-Formula" 3/
put this relationship at 12:7; the formula, essentially, gives the price/cost
relationship needed to maintain equilibrium of supply and demand over the long
term. During the 1952-1970 period the average price of copper (LME, U.S. ex-
ports, U.S. Producers) was $0.38/lb while the average cost of mine production
(excluding royalty and export tax) was $0.21/lb. The "Notman Formula" would
give an average price of $0.36, fairly close to the actual figure. Applying
the formula to the above cost projection of $0.89/lb in current prices for 1980,
we obtain a 1980 copper price of $1.52/lb in current prices.
1/   See Ronald L. Prain, the International Outlook for Copper, American Metal
Market Forum, London, 1971. See also: R. Prain, Copper - New Thoughts
on an Old Theme, in: R. Prain, Selected Papers, Vol. IV, London, 1968.
2/   "Competitive copper" means non-Soviet production on excluding by-product
and co-product copper (i.e., copper produced in mines where other minerals
are predominant or equally import products), mercantilist copper where
decisions on mining depend mainly on criteria other than cost, and mines -
mainly small - with costs unknown. Competitive copper accounts for about
80 percent of total world (excluding CPE's) mine production.
3/   See Arthur Notman:  Economic History of the Copper Industry, in: Copper
Resources of the World; Washington, D.C., 1935.



ANNEX 3-2
Page 32
55.       However, the result of the "Notman Formula" should be considered
with caution and three main reservations must be noted: First, as mentioned
in para. 54, the cost estimate used as basis for the application of the
"Notman Formula" may be overstated, since, inter alia, it includes cost compo-
nents which may increase at less than the average inflation rate (such as
interest, depreciation, royalties, taxes, etc.). Second, the "Notman Formula"
is a statistical relationship which was established during the past decades,
when the international price index increased by 1% - 2% per annum and copper
consumption and industrial production grew rapidly. However, inflation rates
projected at 8.8% per annum during the seventies may have a slowing-down
effect on industrial growth and copper consumption and thus generate a dif-
ferent price/cost relationship. Third, the "Notman Formula" represents an
empirical price/cost relationship in a copper market where major producers
have been private companies whose main objective was profit maximization.
After nationalization of major copper companies in Zaire, Zambia, Chile and
Peru representing nearly 40% of the world's copper mine production, these
state-owned companies may give priority or equal consideration to objectives
such as maximization of export earnings, production and employment. Such a
change of the basic objective may directly alter the empirical "Notman
Formula", i.e. producers may accept a lower price/cost relationship at the
supply/demand equilibrium level.
56.       Over the long term the LME and producer prices tend to reflect pro-
duction cost increases. It can be argued that this long-term trend is not a
smooth rising curve, but rather occurs in major steps. The base of each "step"
reflects a floor price which is only touched in times of serious oversupply
of refined metal. Industry sources 1/ define past floor prices of U.S. $0.10/lb
(1940-45), U.S. $0.20/lb (1953-63), U.S. $0.45/lb (1961-73) and project a floor
price of U.S. $0.70 cents/lb for the next few years.
57.       The above discussion indicates that long-term copper price forecasts
cannot be more than an intelligent guess. The following table summarizes the
range of alternative price projections:

58.       The short-term projection for 1975 ranges from U.S. $0.70/lb to
U.S. $0.80/lb. Such a drop in current copper prices reflects (i) the present
low copper price of U.S.$0.60/lb, (ii) the level and direction of refined metal
stocks and (iii) the projected economic slow down. Reportedly, users of copper
have accumulated large stocks out of fear for a possible long strike in the U.S.
copper industry by mid-1974 which did not materialize. Consequently, demand
for refined copper has been sluggish. The temporary demand suppressing effect
of the release of user stockpiles has been and will continue to be supplemented
by the effects of a worldwide recession. As industrial output in developed
countries is expected to grow at relative low rates in 1975/76 and as indus-
trialization in other regions will not move ahead rapidly enough to offset the
decline in industrialized countries, copper consumption is expected to grow at
a rate of less than 4% per annum in 1975 and 1976. At the same time additional
mine production capacity will become available in various areas and thus should
contribute to a general price decline.
59.       The "high" estimate of U.S. $1.46/lb in 1980 assumes a real price
increase of 2% per annum and an inflation rate of 8.8% per annum after 1975,
i.e., the projection assumes that copper consumption growth will continue at
4.0-4.5% per annum and no significant demand/supply disequilibrium will arise
during the seventies. The "low" projected copper price is based on the assump-
tion that declining copper consumption following a worldwide economic slow
down and a supply surplus from mine expansions which will come on stream before
1979 will create a supply/demand imbalance during the second half of the
seventies. Consequently, profit margins will diminish and medium term copper
prices may be not more than 3-5% above average unit production cost, e.g.,
the 1980 price estimate of U.S. $0.92/lb compares with the 1980 cost estimate
of U.S. $0.89/lb (see para. 54).

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
THE COBALT MART
1. *Cobalt is a silvery-gray metal which resembles nickel in appearance
and in many of its properties. More than 80% of the cobalt used is in some
form of metal and 15Z in cobalt oxides or other chemical compounds. Cobalt
metal is produced and marketed in briquets, granules, squares, broken
cathods, rondelles, fines and powders.
A.  WORLD COBALT RESERVES
2.        Cobalt is found in association with other metals, such as (i) nickel,
chromium and other elements in ultrabasic intrusives, (ii) silver in hydro-
thermal deposits, (iii) with copper in sedimentary deposits, and (iv) with
nickel in laterites formed by weathering of ultrabasic rocks. Normally
cobalt occurs with nickel in a ratio of 1 part cobalt to 20-40 parts of
nickel. The geology of cobalt closely parallels that of nickel in all
occurrences but that of copper.
3.       The principal known reserves of cobalt in the Western world as
compiled by the US Bureau of Mines in 1960 total 1.97 million tons and are  ;
listed below:
Table 1: Principal Cobalt Reserves
Country              Million Lb       000 Tons        x

Mining since 1960 has not depleted any listed reserves significantly. The
grade of reserves ranges from a few hundredths of a percent cobalt in
C    _ian nickel-copper-sulfide ores to 14% cobalt in nickel arsenide ores
in morocco.
4.        Table 1 does not include all the cobalt resources in lower grade
sulfide and laterite deposits as well as in marine deposits. The principal
lateric nickel deposits are in Cuba, the Dominican Republic, Brazil,
Guatemala, Chile, Australia, New Caledonia, the Philippine Islands, and
Indonesia. The lateric nickel reserves in Cuba, which have been estimated
to contain 356 million tons of ore containing 1.3% nickel and 0.1% cobalt
are among the largest in the world. In addition, extensive deposits of
maiganese nodules on the ocean floor represent substantial reserves of
manganese, nickel, copper and cobalt.

B.   COBALT SUPPLY - PAST AND FUTURE DEVELOPMENT
5.        Cobalt has been obtained from three sources:  scrap, land-based
mine production and sales from US Government stockpiles. In the near future,
additional cobalt supply should be available through the exploitation of
manganese nodules obtained from the sea-bed.
Scrap
6.        The production of cobalt from secondary sources is not as large
in proportion to the primary production as is common with many other metals.
Cobalt consumed in non-metallic uses such as pigments, driers, and catalysts
is not recoverable. Most cobalt scrap originates from high-temperature
alloys with smaller quantities generated from magnets.
7.        Data on scrap cobalt is only available in the US.  According to
this information cobalt secondary production accounts for not more than 3%
of total annual requirements, thus representing a minor determinant of
cobalt supply. However, the US exports a significant amount of cobalt
bearing material, mainly to Germany and Belgium, where it is recovered and
refined. These export figures presented in Table 2 below can be considered
a good approximation of the world's (ex CPE) 1/ cobalt scrap supply.
Table 2: US Cobalt Scrap Supply

9.        In 1973 primary production of cobalt reached 25.7 thousand tons,
which was slightly higher than the previous peak figure (1971) and exceeded
the 1972 output by 12.2%. Table 3 shows the increases of mine production
over the past 20 years, and Table 4 below indicates recent output growth.
