Cobalt mining is concentrated in six countries, with Zaire accounting for
nearly 60X of production. Additional small quantities of cobalt-bearing
ores or concentrates are mined in Australia, Austria, Bolivia, Burma,
Chile, Mexico, Spain, Sweden, Norway, etc.
10,       In the majority of mining operations, cobalt is recovered as a
by-product so that the amount of cobalt recovered is largely a function of
the quantities of copper or nickel produced, cobalt production cannot
generally be varied independently.

Minerals Yearbook
11.       Much of the cobalt produced in the world is refined in European
or Canadian plants. GECAMINES refines 65% of its cobalt production locally
to electrolytic cobalt, but the remaining 35%--partially refined cobalt
granules--area treated by the SGM subsidiary Hoboken in Belgium. Hoboken
also refines cobalt alloys or concentrates from Morocco and Zambia, while
the refineries in West Germany, the UK, Norway and Finland treat raw material
imported from Canada, local mine production; and recover scrap.
Stocks
12.       Cobalt has long been considered a strategic and critical material
because it is a vital component of super-alloys and armor-piercing shells.
In the past, the US Government has built up a large inventory of cobalt and
made funds available for exploration of cobalt mineral deposits. US stock-
piles amounted to 28,300 tons as of December 31, 1971; however, the current
objective is to hold only 5,400 tons which implies surplus stock equivalent
to just under one year of current world requirements. No decision has yet
been made on the pace of disposing of the surplus. If sales are not spread
over a long period, they might significantly affect world prices. However,
legislative action in the Congress for authorizing disposal is encountering
delay due to some rethinking about the reliability of raw material supply
sources. Actual disposals from US Government stockpiles are presented in
Table 6 below:

13.       Although cobalt production increased steadily from 15,800 tons
in 1965 to 25,700 tons in 1973, annual production levels were subject to
considerable fluctuations. Table 7 indicates that generally a smaller
amount has been mined whenever notable quantities from stockpiles have
been sold. Traditionally, GECAMINES--as dominating producer--adjusted its
inventories to equal cobalt world demand and supply.
Deep Sea Mining
14.       The discovery of manganese nodules in the sea bed in enormous
quantities is likely to change the cobalt supply structure significantly
and relatively more than for other non-ferrous metals. For the proposed
sea-bed operations, although larger quantities of both manganese and nickel
would be extracted, the amount of by-product cobalt recovered would repre-
sent a far greater portion of current land-based production than would be
the case for any other metal. It is estimated, for example, that one
commercial deep-sea mining operation could add each year up to 30% to current
cobalt production, while comparable portions for manganese or nickel would
be less than 5%.
15.       During the past 5 years several companies 1/  have started to
explore the nodule resource. But none of these firms have chosen and tested
mine sites, mining equipment and processing plants, and theoretically would
not be ready to move rapidly to commercial production without an additional
lead time at least 7 years. Further the legal status of ocean sea mining is
still unresolved and continues to be under review by scheduled future UN
conferences on the Law of the Sea. At this point in time, projections of
future cobalt supply are speculative because of three major uncertainties,
i.e., (i) the metal output per ton of dry nodules, (ii) the size of marine
mining operations, and (iii) the entry dates of sea-bed mining enterprises
into commercial production.
1/   Deepsea Ventures, Inc./Tenneco Inc.; Hughes; Kennecott Group; Ocean
Resources Inc.; International Nickel Company; Sumitomo Group/MITI; AMR
(West Germany); CNEXO-Societe Le Nickel.

16.       The first generation of nodule mines are believed to be able to
obtain about a 95 percent metallurgical recovery. Although manganese nodules
vary in metal content and quality, a typical dry nodule would probably in-
clude about 0.21 percent cobalt content per weight. Therefore, one million
tons of dry nodules would provide about 2,000 tons of cobalt metal. 1/
17.       Economies of scale are considered to be strong at the processing
stage requiring a size of plant between three and four million tons of dry
nodules. On the other hand, an optimum size of capacity for a mining rig
is estimated at between one and two million tons of dry nodules annually.
Most systems would, therefore, be geared to the processing of three to four
million tons annually from 2-3 mining rigs.
18.       The timing of entry into sea-bed mining operations will be influenced
by:
(i) the degree of readiness of the experimenting enterprises to
start commercial exploitation;
(ii) the solution to the deep-sea mining rights question; and
(iii) the extent of control over the pace of nodule mining imposed
by the International Sea-bed Authority. j/
Several UN studies have been carried out projecting total cobalt production
from the sea-bed to reach between 3,000 to 20,000 tons in 1980; however,
these express general consensus that commercial nodule exploitation will not
start during the next two years. Table 8 on page 9 gives a possible range
of cobalt supply from deep-sea mining, as estimated by the mission.
1/   There is some difference in opinion on the amount of cobalt metal recovered
from dry nodules. A UNCTAD study used data from a 1971 United Nations
publication (A/AC.138/36) thus putting the production of cobalt at 2,900
tons per one million tons of dry nodules. The UN latest study revised
the figure to 2,000 tons of cobalt. The latter estimate was based on a
combination of data from various recent sources-see Third Conference
on the Law of the Sea-op. cit. pp. 27-28. The lower figure seems to be
nearer the likely outcome.
2/   The regime is currently under negotiation.  The basic guidelines envisage
to control the rate of nodule mining as a function of nickel demand
increases.

1985 the African producers, and in particular GECAMINES, will probably lose
their dominant market position, with deep-sea operations accounting for 16%
of projected cobalt mine production.

21.       About 70% of cobalt consumption in the US is for metallic uses
while non-metallic uses, pigments and salts account for the remainder. High-
temperature alloys (superalloys) has been the most important end-use of cobalt
since the Second World War, accounting for appximately 20% of total consump-
tion in the US. The important properties of superalloys: their significant
strength at high temperatures, resistance to corrosion by oxidation, and
resistance to contamination by alkali and acid, have made them particularly
essential for jet engines, rotor blades and other parts which are subjected
to stress at high temperatures. There are basically four types of super-
alloys in general use: iron-base alloys with chromium and nickel, chromium-
nickel-cobalt-iron alloys, nickel-base alloys and cobalt-base alloys. Cobalt
and nickel superalloys have generally comparable properties and can therefore
substitute for one another to a great extent. However, there are special
properties attached to alloys of each group which would make them particularly
suitable for certain, but rather limited applications. It is believed that
the base for cobalt alloys could expand significantly at lower prices. In
the post-war period until the end of the 1950's, consumption of cobalt in
high-temperature alloys increased by about 6.6% annually but the rate decreased
between 1960 and 1971 to under 2%.
22.       The second important use of cobalt is in the permanent magnetic
field, currently accounting for about 17% of US consumption. Alnico permanent
magnets all contain cobalt. The principal use of these magnets, taking
between 75% and 80% of the Alnico market, is in loudspeakers installed in
televisions, radios and stereos. The remainder part is used in motors and
generators. An innovative variation has been introduced to combine cobalt
in powder form with other materials so even stronger magnets with good
stability at high temperatures could be obtained. However, ceramic magnets
have been increasingly cutting into the market for cobalt-base magnets.
The result is that the annual growth rate of the US market for cobalt magnets,



ANNEX 3-3
Page 12
which was about 6.5% between 1948 and 1960, has since then reversed its
direction showing instead a decline of about 1% annually. Even at a consid-
erable lower price, cobalt may not regain the market from ceramics.
23.       Cobalt services a number of other metallic uses, each of which
accounts for a small share of total consumption, although taken together they
are responsible for one-third of the end-uses. 1/ These include the use of
cobalt in various steel alloys beside superalloys (including three basic
categories: speed, maraging and tool steels), cemented carbides, and as
seals of metals to various non-metal materials. Cobalt use in steel alloys
commands the relatively highest tonnage of this group where its consumption
has been rising. Accounting for about one-third of cobalt used in steel
alloys, high speed steel is important as a cutting-tool material for machining
various metals at high speeds. Most of these high-speed steels contain
tungsten and molybdenum. Cobalt cannot directly substitute for either, but
indirectly a molybdenum high-speed steel with a cobalt addition can replace
a tungsten high-speed steel.
24.       It also enters into a number of other steel alloys:  maraging
steels and tool steels, of which the former are applied particularly in
the aerospace industry and production of missiles. In addition, cobalt
is almost indispensable in machine tools as the metal matrix that binds
various carbides together to form cutting tools. This latter market has
been notably growing. Consumption of all metallic uses grew at about 7%
annually since 1960, which is the highest growth rate for any type of cobalt
consumption in the US (although it declined from an average growth rate of
12% annually in the post-war period until 1960), Table 11.
25.       Non-metallic uses of cobalt enjoy a dynamic and potentially
rapidly growing market. Cobalt is a very powerful drier; it acts much
quicker than its substitutes in this field: Manganese and lead. It is
essential in paints, varnishes, printing inks and related materials.
Furthermore, as a catalyst for desulfurizing petroleum and coal, it is
expanding at a significant pace. Its future prospects in this field are
quite promising, particularly as low-sulfur crude petroleum is likely to
be relatively scarce given future demand, and as various government's
legislation on smoke control and air pollution takes effect. In the latter
field, the use of cobalt as a catalyst in the automobile exhaust system is
crucial.
26.       As a result of the 1969 nickel strike in Canada, cobalt was used
in electro-plating as a substitute for nickel for a period of time. It is,
however, unlikely that it becomes a permanent replacement for straight
nickel plating. The disadvantage of cobalt in this field is its inability
1/ See an extensive account of these in James Burrows: Cobalt: An Industry
Analysis, Heath Lexington, Chapter 3.

Cobalt Consumption Pattern
27.       Cobalt consumption is concentrated in highly developed countries.
As indicated in Tables 12 and 13, in 1972 the US accounted for 39.0 of the
world cobalt consumption, Japan for 14.0%, the UK for 11.5% and West Germany
for 9.5%.
Table 13:  Cobalt Consumption Growth
Growth Rate
Country                            1972 Consumption              1950-71
(000 tons)   (%)                (z)
USA                                   8.9       39.0                3.4
Western Europe                        8.5       34.2                6.5
Japan                                 3.2       14.0                9.1
World (ex CPE)                       22.8      100.0                5.8
Overall, cobalt consumption increased by 5.8% per-annum during the 1950-71
period.  In the USA, growth rates averaged only 3.4%; however, cobalt con-
sumption increased by 4.1% during the 1960-71 period due to the demand
stimulating effects of the Vietnam war and general economic prosperity.
Cobalt consumption is closely related to industrial production. Regression
analysis covering the 1952-71 period indicates that cobalt consumption grew
1.05 times as fast as industrial production, while it grew only 0.96 times
as fast during the 1960-72 period.
Projected Cobalt Consumption
28.       Future cobalt consumption will depend primarily on the pace of
industrial growth in advanced countries and the possibility that cobalt
would substitute for various non-ferrous metals at significantly lower
prices. Table 14 presents cobalt consumption projections until 1982 under
three different hypothesis of consumption and industrial production growth
rates.

years, it is reasonable to assume that average cobalt consumption will not
grow faster than during the past two decades. Hypotheses II and III,
therefore, represent the most likely estimates projecting cobalt consumption
of about 35,000 tons in 1980 and 39,000 in 1982.

GECAMINES fix a price which is invariably adopted by other producers. 1/

production of other countries, sales of cobalt from the US Government stock-
pile and operating cost increases. Cobalt prices since 1950 are presented
in Table 15. The high price level in the early 1950's and a relatively

ZAIRE: CECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
MARKETING ARRANGEMENTS
1.        When GECAMINES was established as Zairian-owned state company in
1967, SGM (Societe Generale des Minerais) agreed to continue the commercializa-
tion of GECAMINES' products on a fee basis and to provide a system of export
financing. Marketing fees range between 0.8-1 .0% of sales prices depending on
sale volume for copper and between 2.9-4.2% for cobalt. In addition, SGM
charges an agency fee of 0.5%. SGM maintains its own sales agencies in major
consumer countries or has acquired participation in foreign sales organizations.
Refining
2.        Since GECAMINES' refining and transformation capacity is not yet
sufficient to produce refined quality metal products in Zaire, SGM continues
to treat on toll part of GECAMINES' copper and cobalt products in Belgium by
its subsidiary Metallurgie Hoboken-Overpelt - Electrolytic refinery Olen.
In 1972, GECAMINES shipped to Belgium for further processing (i) 210,500 tons
of blister copper or copper cathodes which is equivalent to 48.5% of total
sales, and (ii) 1,804 tons of cathode or white alloy cobalt equivalent to 14%
of cobalt sales. Table 1 gives a distribution of copper exports by country
and refining operation; all production of germanium, silver and gold results
from refining in Olen (Metallurgie Hoboken - Overpelt). The Olen refinery has
a capacity of 400,000 tons per year of refined copper covering a wide range
of specifications and is able to refine about 15,000 tons per year of cobalt.
Contracts
3.        Although the clientele of GECAMINES' products (trade mark UMKH) has
been relatively stable, SGM has no long-term contracts or even contracts of
more than one year. An exception is the sales arrangement between GECAMINES,
SGM and Metallurgie Hoboken - Overpelt, which was modified on April 10, 1974.
Accordingly, GECAMINES agreed to supply Metallurgie Hoboken - Overpelt with
80,000 tons of copper cathodes p.a. until 1978, 150,000 tons of blister copper
p.a. until 1980, and decreasing by not more than 50,000 tons of blister copper
p.a. thereafter.
4.        As a result of this agreement, GECAMINES expects to diminish its
dependence on the Belgian copper market (23% of total copper sales in 1973)
and strengthen its position in the Brazilian and West German markets (see
distribution of sales in Tables 2 and 3). All sales contracts concluded by
SGM for GECAMINES stipulate a two months' pricing period based on LME quotations.
Future Marketing Arrangement
5.        On April 10, 1974, the Government of Zaire and GECAMINES signed an
agreement which outlines the future marketing arrangements. The agreement
stipulates:



ANNEX 3-4
Page 2
(i) SGM will transfer its exclusive marketing responsibility
for GECAMINES' products to a wholly Zairian-owned company
for the cotmercialization of mineral products. This
company will operate as GECAMINES' agent.
(1i) The new marketing company (SOZACOM) will take headquarters
in Kinshasa. It will be established with SGM's technical
assistance and should take over all marketing activities by
March 31, 1976, or later if requested by SOZACOM.
(iii)  For its services, SGM receives marketing fees according to
the extent of its intervention and payments under a separate
contract regulating the technical assistance provided for the
conception, organization, recruitment and staff training of
SOZACOM.
(iv) For SGM's know-how, SGM will receive 400 million FB (US$10 million)
at the final transfer of the marketing responsibility to SOZACOM.
6.        SGM has been asked to present to the Government of Zaire an organiza-
tional, staffing and training plan for the newly created marketing organization,
SOZACOM (Societe Zairoise de Commercialisation des Minerais). SOZACOM has
been created on July 10, 1974 by decree No. 74-010, and Mr. Zola has been
appointed General Manager.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
Technical Description of the Expansion Project
A.   Introduction
1.        The Mashamba and Dikuluwe ore deposits are located in the Western
Group within Gecamines' Copper Concession. It is one of the larger known
deposits remaining within the Concession.
2.        The mining of the deposits, together with the concentration, smelt-
ing, and refining facilities will result in major adjustments in material
flows. The effect in these changes is shown in Annex 4-1, page 10. A significant
effect is the treatment of lower grade oxide ore stockpiled during the mining
of the older open pits.
B.   Geology and Ore Reserves
3.        The Dikuluwe and Mashamba deposits are typical of the stratiform
type found within Gecamines' concession. The two mineralized bands, each
approximately 12 m thick, are separated by a 20 m band of waste.
4.        Roughly the shape of a truncated basin, the Dikuluwe deposit dips
at 700 near surface. The dip gradually lessens to flatten out some 500
meters below surface.
5.        A section approximately 1,500 m long outcrops on surface.  As a
result, some 10 million tons are weathered and altered to oxides. The depth
of oxidation shows considerable variation along the length of the outcrop
but generally averages 100 meters.
6.        The Mashamba deposit, adjacent to the Dikuluwe, shows a more complex
geology. The general form is an anticline. It has also been subjected to
a greater degree of folding and faulting. Diamond drilling indicates that
there is significant interruption in the ore formation in the flat section
at depth. The outcrop on surface is considerably smaller than the Dikuluwfe
with less ore as oxides (approximately 6 million tons).
7.        Some 75,000 m of diamond drilling have been undertaken to evaluate
the deposits. In the portions near surface, the diamond drilling is on a
25 m pattern. This close spacing is utilized to determine the type and grade
of ore with a high degree of assurance.  It has been found necessary in
order to ensure effective mine planning of the type of ore fed to the
concentrator.  The deeper portions of the ore zone have been drilled on a
100 m pattern. Closer drilling for mine planning will be undertaken as
required.



ANNIEX 4-1
Page 2
8.        The deposits are well outlined geologically.  The background data
and knowledge of these deposits built un over the years by Gecamines permits
them to make an effective evaluation. The allowances in ore reserve calcul-
ation for dilution, sampling error, and cut-off grade are based on experience.
Review of this data by the mission indicates that the allowances are conserva-
tive. The cut-off grade is high. However the usually clear-cut demarcation
between ore and waste would preclude any but small additions to the ore reserves
if a lower figure were used.
9.        Total ore reserves for the Dikuluwe-Mashanba deposits are 171 million
tons grading 4.1% copper and 0.3% cobalt, sufficient for forty years mining.
While the deposit will probably all be mined by open pit methods, some 66
million tons, sufficient for 16 years at the projected rate of mining, is con-
tained in the presently defined pit limits.
C.   Main Production Facilities
1.   Mining
10.       The ore zones outcrop over a significant length on surface.  They
are therefore suitable for the open pit mining method chosen. The mining
operation will be similar to other open pits in the world.
11.       A small stream, the Luilu, and a tributary, the Kabulungu, join
together and flow over the Dikuluwe deposit. Two small earthfill dams and
4,000 m of diversion canal are being undertaken. The dars are approximately
300 m and 150 m long respectively.
12.       Other preparatory work includes pre-stripping of 8 million m3 of
waste from the two pits, construction of access roads, installation of
electrical distribution system and pit drainage system. One million tons
of tailings in an old storage basin must also be moved.
13.       Large scale equipment has been chosen for the mining operation.
15 cu. yd. electric shovels will be used with 100 t electric wheel trucks.
Gecamines has been standardizing on this size of equipment. In addition
to being proven in mining operations around the world, it is equipment with
which Gecamines has considerable experience.
14.       The pit slopes are generally at 40°.  However, the slopes do vary
according to rock type. In the footwall of the ore, 45o or steeper slopes
are normal whereas in the sandy shales encountered in one area, the slopes
may go as low as 16°. The study of pit slopes and slope stability represents
one of the more important activities of the mining research department.
15.       The mining sequence is established from the geological data of ore
type and grade. This data, divided into small blocks, is programmed for
Gecamines' computer, together with data on mining requirements and capabilities,
to determine the most economical sequence.



ANNEX 4-1
Page 3
16.       The Dikuluwe-Mashaiba open pits will supply four million tons of
ore per year by 1977. The Dikuluwe open pit will actually start producing
in 1975 at an annual rate of 750,000 tons of oxide ore.
17.       The ore would be crushed to -5" in semi-mobile 700 TPH jaw crushers
located at the exits of each pit. It will then be conveyed to stockpiles at
the new DIMA (the name comes from Dikuluvwe Mashamba) concentrator. The
conveyor from the Dikuluwe pit is 3,200 m long while the one from Mashamba
is 1,200 m long.
2.   DIMA Concentrator
18.       The DIMA concentrator will be located adjacent to the existing
Kamoto concentrator. The basic flowsheet and type of equipment will be
similar to that used at Kamoto IT. Two circuits -- one for oxide ores and
the other for mixed ores -- are projected for the concentrator.
19.       The ore conveyed from the primary crushers would be discharged
onto stockpiles of 13,000 t each for the mixed and oxide ores. This is
sufficient for 2.5 days operation of the concentrator.
20.       Each tyDe of ore, reclaimed from its stockpile, would be ground
in 32' x 14' autogenous grinding mills. Following classification the
oversize would be fed to 10.5' x 16' ball mills. Equipped with variable
speed motors, the ball mills can be adjusted to suit the hardness of the
ore. The output would be 85% -200 mesh (-0.07 mm) for the mixed ore and
75% -200 mesh for the oxides.
21.       Following grinding, the ores are treated in the flotation section.
The use of large (300 cil. ft.) flotation cells is proposed. In the oxide
circuit, a bulk rough concentrate is produced. This involves flotation of
a bulk concentrate by sulfidization, followed by conditioning and flotation
of another concentrate. These bulk concentrates are combined and cleaned
up by flotation to produce a final concentrate. The rejects from cleaner
flotation are returned to the bulk flotation circuit. The final concentrate
will grade approximately 24% copper. Recovery is expected to be 84 to 86%.
22.       The mixed circuit is somewvhat more complex but follows the same
principles. The hulk sulphide concentrate and oversize from the bulk dolomite
oxide concentrate are reground in a 10.5' x 16' ball mill. This is then
treated in a second bulk flotation circuit. The bulk sulphide concentrates
are combined and subjected to two cleaner flotation stages. The bulk dolomitic
concentrates are put through one cleaner stage. The final concentrates will
grade approximately 50% copper for the sulphides and 18% copper for the
dolomitic oxides. Total recovery should exceed 84%.
23.       The testing program by Gecamines Metallurgical Research Group
has been thorough and comprehensive. The various types of ores from the
two deposits have been tested in the company's laboratory. Composite samples



ANNEX 4-1
Page 4
were also prepared and tested. Each of the various operating parameters --
grinding fineness, flotation, reagents were well examined. The tests indicate
that the proposed flowsheet is suited to the ore. The three types of con-
centrates produced will be pumped 10 km to the plant at Luilu.
3. Flash Smelter
24.       The sulphide concentrates, pumped to the Luilu site in three 10"
pipelines, will be thickened and filtered to lower the water content below
14%. They would then be conveyed to stockpiles.
25.       Reclaimed from the stockpile, the sulDhide concentrates are then
dried. Synthetic gas will be the fuel used in the drying furnace. The
dried concentrates, together with flux, synthetic gas, and oxygen enriched
air are fed into the top of the flash smelting furnace.
26.       The molten copper from the flash smelter will be transferred to
an anode furnace for reduction, and pouring as copper anodes.
27.       The slag (containing 6% copper and 6% cobalt) from the flash smelter
will be treated in an electric furnace to first produce blister copper. In
a second electric furnace, the slag is retreated with a flux (lime), other
slag waste, and a reducer to produce a copper-cobalt-iron alloy. This alloy
will be granulated and packed in containers for export.
28.       Another electric anode furnace, and casting wheel will be incor-
porated in this plant, and will melt the used copper anodes from the Liulu
hydrometallurgical plant and other plant scrap, and re-cast it as copper
anodes.
29.       The flash smelter proposed is based on the Outokumpu process.
Developed by Outokumpu oy for the treatment of copper sulphide concentrates,
the first smelter was commissioned in Finland in 1949. There are now over
20 smelters in operation or under construction. While most of these are
for use with copper concentrates, a few are treating nickel concentrates,
or other sulphide concentrates. Aside from the usual adjustments in early
operations, those smelters in operation are functioning well.
30.       The process is particularly well suited for Gecamines concentrates.
The high grade concentrates with few metallic impurities permit the direct
production of blister copper at 98+% copper. As a result, the process is
simplified and much cheaper. Heat is given off in the reaction, resulting
in lower fuel consumption. The flash smelter also yields a smelter gas con-
taining 14-18% sulphur dioxide. The use of the S02 to produce sulphuric acid
will enable Gecamines to become self-sufficient in its acid requirements.
The production of acid and electrostatic precipitation of dusts results in
a very clean gas discharge.
31.       Following preliminary testing by Gecamines Metallurgical laboratory,
Outokumpu was contacted for more extensive testing. Following initial work,
600 tons of concentrates were treated in Outokumpu's 25 ton per ton pilot



ANNEX 4-1
Page 5
plant. This covered the range of concentrate grades Gecamiens expects to
produce. The tests confirmed the feasibility of producing blister copper
directly.
32.       Gecamines has signed an engineering agreement with Outokumpo Oy for
design, engineering, and start-up for the flash smelter furnace and associated
equipment. Outokumpo has an excellent reputation for the quality of their
services.
4.   Electro-Refinery
33.       The copper anodes from the flash smelter will be refined electro-
lytically. The basic principles are similar to those in Gecaaine's existing
electrolytic circuits. The copper, from the anodes, is deposited on the
cathode as relatively pure metal. The impurities remain on the anode or
accumulate in the bottom of the electrolytic cell as "mud".
34.       Following a study of alternatives, the electro-refining process
was chosen. The blister copper, produced in Outokumpu's pilot plant, was
returned to Gecamines for semi-industrial scale tests. It was cast into
anodes and subjected to electrolysis in a special test section of the Shituru
hydrometallurgical plant. Although the anodes were of low physical quality,
high quality cathode copper was produced. The opeiating parameters were
also quite satisfactory. The plant results should be better than indicated
in the test.
D.   Modifications to Existing Metallurgical Plant
35.       Since introduction of the flash smelting unit for treatment of
sulphide ores will release capacity in the Luilu hydrometallurgical plant,
an increased quantity of oxide concentrates can be processed. Therefore
provision must be made to handle these oxide concentrates. At the same time,
some improvements in the existing circuit would increase capacity by 15,000
tons to 190,000 t.
36.       Oxide concentrates arrive at Luilu by pipeline from Kamoto (and
Dima when it is operational), and by rail from Kolwezi. The concentrates
from Kamoto and Dima, must be then dewatered by thickening and filtering.
The concentrates would be transferred directly to the head of the leaching
circuit after thickening or filtered and placed in a stockpile. Four storage
basins of 50 x 26 x 3 meters for the concentrates pumped from Xamoto and
Dima have also been provided for. Required equipment includes 5 - 18 m
diameter ihickeners, 1 - 22 m thickener, and 4 - 11.5' x 17' rotobelt filters,
3 - 100 m pulp tanks, and a 45,000 ton covered stockpile with a stacker and
reclaimer.
37.       The improvements in the leaching circuit are mainly in the washing
and clarification section. 3 new 22 m diameter thickners and 4 - 11.5' x 17'
rotobelt filters are required in this section. Some rationalization and
rearrangement of the existing equipment would also be undertaken.



ANNEX 4-1
Page 6
38.       To reduce losses in the waste filtration section, double filtration
would be iniroduced. This would require 4 - 11.5' x 17' rotobelt filters,
and a 200 m pulp tank. A third selenium column is also required.
39.       In the selective precipitation section, additional filter capacity
will be installed. This is in the denickellng, and dezincing section pri-
marily. Two 14' x 14' filters and two 60 m drum filters are necessary.
40.       Other changes to provide for the increase in capacity include an
additional thickener and filter for the denickeling section, and additions
to the preparation of lime.
E.  Ancillary Facilities
1.   Oxygen Plant
41.       To provide oxygen enriched air for the flash smelter and coal
gasification plant, a 350 ton per day oxygen plant is to be constructed at
Luilu. Oxygen will also be supplied to the existing fluo-solid roasters.
42.       While the design of the plant is to be supplied by the contractor,
it is expected that it will be similar to the plant in operation at the
Lubumbashi smelter. Key equipment, such as the oxygen compressors, will be
duplicated to ensure a continuous supply to the flesh smelter.
2.   Sulphuric Acid Plant
43.       To purify the gases from the flash smelter and provide sulphuric
acid for the hydrometallurgical plant, an acid plant will be installed to
treat the smelter gases. The traditional catalytic process will be used.
Acid production will be approximately 300 tons per day.
44.       The smelter gases containing 14-17% SO2, pass through a waste heat
boiler and an electrostatic precipitator at the smelter. Thereby 99% of
the dust will be removed and the temperature is lowered to 350'. The gases
will be further cleaned up and dried at the acid plant before the production
of sulphuric acid. Sulphur dioxide recovery is expected to be around 98%.
As a result, the smelter gases will contain few pollutants on discharge to
the atmosphere.
3.   Coal Gasification
45.       In order to evaluate alternate sources of energy for the flash
smelter and auxiliary facilities, Gecamines undertook a preliminary study of
five possibilities in early 1973. The results indicated that gasification
of the Luena lignite was competitive, and offered other advantages. The
changes in oil and other energy prices since the study have improved the
economics. It also underlined another advantage, in that fuel costs would
be free from outside events, and under Gecamines control.
46.       Following the preliminary study, a sample of Luena lignite was
sent to Lurgi in Germany. This testing confirmed the suitability of the
Luena coal for gasification.



ANNEX 4-1
Page 7
47.       The use of Luena lignite will require an expansion of the mine
from approximately 110,000 tons of coal per year to 200,000 tons. The washing
plant will be put on a two shift per day basis. The coal would be transported
290 km by rail to Luilu, and transferred to a stockpile. Reclaimed, the coal
will be crushed to - 30 mn and screened.  The - 5 mm fraction will be shipped
out for use elsewhere in Gecamineo.  The -30 + 5 mm portion will be conveyed
to the coal gasification unit.
48.       The coal gasification plant will be based on the Lurgi process.
This process is one of the more advanced of the existing technologies for
gasification.
49.       The unit will treat approximately 200 tons of coal per day, and
produce 8,800 Nm3 of gas per hour with a calorific value of 3,800 Kcal/Nm3.
4.   Limestone Qlarry and Kiln
50.       The use of lime as a flux in the flash smelter and for Ph control
in the hydrometallurgical plants will require an expansion in the Kakontwe
quarry and calcining plant. An increase of approximately 25,000 tons of lime
per year will be required.
51.       The Kakontwe quarry is located 6 km from Likasi in the Central
Group.  Presently 120,000 t of lime and 8,000 tons of calcium hydrate are
produced yearly. A third kiln will be added to achieve the required produc-
tion, in conjunction with increased quarry output.
F.   Administrative and Industrial Infrastructure
52.       Since the Western Group of mines presently accounts for 65% of
Gecamines' production, the basic infrastructure required for the expansion
unit already exists. In most cases, however, additions or modifications in
the existing facilities will be required. Transport, power supply and tele-
communications are covered in Annex 2-6, para. 2-22.
1.   Internal Transport
53.       The major items required are re-routing of some of the railroad
spurs and roads within the existing plant site at Luilu. The cost of this
work is estimated at Z 1.05 million.
2.   Water Supply
54.       Water for the expansion project will be pumped from the Luilu river.
Expansion of the existing pumping and a new water treatment plant will cost
Z 1.03 million. The water consumption is estimated at 3,400 m3 per hour.
55.       The water treatment plant will include sections for filtration (850
m3/hr), softening (550 n3/h), and demineralization (80 m3/h).



ANNEX 4-1
Page 8
3.   Electrical Distribution
56.       The main sub-station at Luilu will require reinforcement with the
installation of 8 km of 220 KV line from the Western Group HT centre to which
SNEL delivers power. The required 200/110 KV transformers will be moved
from the Seke dam. In addition new 110/15 KV transformers will be required
at Luilu, and at DIMA.
57.       Gecamines will be responsible for installation of the distribution
lines to each of the plants in the project.
58.       The total cost of the electrical installation is estimated at Z 2.98
million.
4.   Ratonto Aggregrate Quarry and Plant
59.       To provide aggregates for the concrete required in the project and
to meet future needs, Gecamines plans to 3double output of the aggregate quarry
and plant. Output is presently 12,000 m per month. Cost of the mining equip-
ment - trucks and 4" drills-is estimated at t 53,000.
60.       The plant, consisting of crushing, and wet screening equipment, pro-
duces four products: 50/25 mm for ballast; and 25/10 mm gravel, 10/2 mm gravel
and sand for concrete aggregate.  The estimated cost is t 1.58 million to double
output.
5.   Workshops
61.       The "Ateliers Quest" central workshops will be expanded to meet the
increased volume of maint nance. Total cost is estimated at Z 2.37 million.
Included are some 3,900 m of building and equipment - 2 large lathes, 1 radial
drill, etc.
62.       In addition, expansion of the Luilu workshop for the metallurguical
facilities is also planned. Total cost is estimated at t 0.92 million.
6.   Other
63.       Various other facilities, such as expansion of office space, com-
pressed air capacity, gasoline storage are also planned at Luilu. Estimated
cost is t 3.75 million.
G.   Ecology
64.       Within the Zairian context, the expansion project is satisfactory
from an ecological point of view. The deposit is located in rolling savannah
land which is sparsely populated and of low value agriculturally. The impact
of open pit mining is, of course, considerable, but the land area involved
is quite restricted. Waste from the open pit will be dumped in the area south
and east of the pit.



ANNEX 4-1
Page 9
65.       Two small streams, the Luilu and Kabulunga, join together and flow
over the deposit. Two small dams and a diversion canal, which should be com-
plete early in 1975, will direct the streams around the open pit. These struc-
tures are well designed and should pose no safety hazard.
66.       The concentrator rejects (tailings) will be pumped to a site south
east of the open pits. The earthfill retaining dam, to be constructed in three
phases with the first phase completed in mid 1974, has been designed on a sound
engineering basis. The contractor has been closely supervised, and soil tests
are undertaken regularly. No safety hazard should result from this dam. The
water overflow, based on samples from the adjacent Kamoto concentrator, is of
better quality than the intake water. The pH, being slightly alkali, precludes
dissolution of copper and other metals in high concentrations.
67.       Gases from the flash smelter will be treated in waste heat boilers
and electro-static precipitators before entering the sulphuric acid plant. As
a result, 98% of the sulphur dioxide and 99% of the dust particles will be re-
moved. It is expected that the gases will meet the standards set by the U.S.
EPA for 1975.
68.       Cooling water for the smelter furnaces will be circulated in a closed
circuit so that only make-up water and minor water usage, such as slag granula-
tion will be discharged from the plant.
69.       The bulk of the process fluids in the electro-refinery operate in
closed circuit with the existing hydro-metallurgical plant at Luilu. Therefore
waste discharges from the refinery are minimal.
H.   Social Infrastructure
70.       Investment in social infrastructure include i) housing, (ii) exten-
sion of existing community facilities in the Kolwezi area, i.e. 6 sport cen-
ters, 2 schools and 3 village centers (iii) extension of the Kolwezi hospital
and the rehabilitation of two squatter settlements. For a detailed enumera-
tion of the planned investments see Annex 5-1, para 9.



ANNEX 4-1
Page 10
71.       Most of the construction workers for the project are expected to
come from two squatter settlements adjacent to Luilu-Tshamudenda and Mwadi.
Adequate houses do exist in these settlements, but water supply and sanitary
conditions have been below standard. To improve the living conditions of its
potential workers, GECAMINES will - as part of the project - install 17 public
fountains and drainage ditches in Tshamudenda and 45 public fountains and
drainage ditches in Mwadi. The work will be executed by GECAMINES' construc-
tion department and will start in early 1975.
72.       The project includes the construction of 2,000 houses for the newly
hired workers of the expansion and 330 new staff houses to meet the future
requirement of the Western Group. The construction of 400 houses for workers
at Luilu and 330 houses for staff near Mutoshi is underway. Additional 1,600
worker houses including infrastructure and communal facilities will be built
before the end of 1977. Two locations for this new cite are being investi-
gated and a decision will be taken shortly.

cost.  All major items will be supplied by U.S. firms and financed by the
company's funds. GECAMINES has already ordered all the major mining equip-
ment, so that the above costs represent actual 1973-74 prices. Items valued
at X 7.2 million had been delivered by mid-1974.
Civil Works (Foundations)
2.        Civil works cost for the new plants includes construction of the
foundations and has been estimated at 9 12.47 million including a foreign
exchange component of about 0 7.89 million (64%). The contractor for the
plant foundation contract will be selected following international competitive
bidding.
Plant Unit (Items 2-6, 9-12, 15)
3.        The new installations have been divided into plant units which
represent procurement packages. Following the Bank's procurement guidelines
tenders have been invited separately for each plant unit which includes engi-
neering and design; equipment supply and erection; building construction ex-
clusive of foundations; supervision and start-up. Although each contractor
will have full responsibility for the execution of his plant unit, GECAMINES
will provide some common services such as equipment, assistance in transport,
recruiting, housing and social services for the labor force and staff, and
maintain control and coordinating functions.

project estimated at 91.13 million.
Physical Contingencies
11.       Physical contingency allowances reflect GECAMINES' past experi-
ence with various project components. Consequently, no contingencies have
been added either for pre-operating expenses, mining equipment, or for other
already ordered items. The applied contingencies rate ranges from 5% to 20%
of equipment, erection and building cost totalling X12.25 million which is
equivalent to 7.9% of total capital cost.
Price Escalation
12.       Cost estimates for all plant items are based on either 1974 orders
for mining equipment, houses and miscellaneous equipment items, October 1974
bids or June 1974 quotations of interested suppliers for all plant units or
on GECAMINES updated estimate for all remaining equipment and construction
items. Price escalations of 1.2% per month in 1974, 15% p.a. in 1975, 12%
p.a. in 1976 and 10% per annum have been used and added to the capital cost
in line with the projected project implementation and disbursement schedule
(see Annexes 4-3 and 5-4). Price escalations average 20.2% of total capital
cost plus physical contingencies.

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
ASSUMPTIONS FOR FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
1.        The financial projections are based on the expansion plan outlined
in Annex 4-1. Production start-up of the expansion facilities are scheduled
during late 1977. After the start-up of the concentrator in August the company
produces a saleable product, even if the flash smelter will take up production
only at the end of 1977. In order to adequately reflect the Company's future
cash position, all projections are given in nominal terms or current prices,
however, the rate of return calculations are based on projections in real terms.
2.        The total expansion production will be exported assuming the
following current prices:
Metal LME Prices             SGM Quotations
Year                    Copper                       Cobalt      _
FB/ton       IJS$/lb        FB/ton        US$/lb
1975            65,000         0.73          315,000        3.57
1976             67,600        0.76          342,700        3.88
1977             70,980        0.80          372,900        4.22
1978             74,555        0.84          405',700       4.60
1979            78,260         0.88          426,000        4.83
1980            82,160         0.92          447,300        5.04
1981            86,255         0.97          469,600        5.32
1982            90,610         1.02          493,000        5.60
1983            95,095         1.07          493,000        5.60
1984            99,840         1.12          493,000        5.60
1985           104,845         1.17          493,000        5.60
3.        Sales Revenues are given net of processing and marketing expenses
incurred by SGM and directly deducted from cif sales receipts. As shown in
Annexes 3-4, in the past processing and marketing expenses averaged 7.8%
and 23% of copper and cobalt gross sales respectively. Sales revenues as
given in GECAMINES income statement do not correspond to FOB sales receipts
since they still include transport cost, export financing charges etc. of
gross sales for copper and cobalt respectively.
4.        Operating Costs for the expansion project are confidential infor-
mation and are available in the Industrial Projects Department. All costs
have been projected in real terms, and then inflated by the following annual
rates:



ANNEX 6-1
Page 2
1975     1976      1977      1978    1979-85
(% increase per annum)
Labor                         4        10       3         7           4
Materials, Supplies          12         8        7         7           5
External Supplies and
Services                   9         9        5         7           5
Transport                    10         8       5         5           5
These rates are based on prevailing local and related world inflation rates
and have been found to adequately cover expected increases in operating costs.
Future productivity increases have been taken into account in the projections
of quantifiable cost elements such as labor, fuel, chemical reagents, power,
etc. Major costs such as fuel and power are not subsidized and GECAMINES
imports nearly 70% of materials, supplies and spare parts which are charged
at world market prices.
5.         Labor Costs are based upon the following labor rates including
salaries, social charges, housing and home leave (for expatriates).
1974
Expatriate staff              Z2,100/mqnth
African staff                 Z  520/month
African labor                 Z   70/month
Expatriate staff is almost exclusively made up of engineers, economists and
administrators; after 1980 a greater increase in African staff is projected
corresponding to a decline in expatriate staff (See Annex 2-7, Table 2 for
details).
6.         Freight and Loading Costs - It is assumed that the "voie nationale"
route Shaba-Matadi will be used to capacity on a priority basis, followed by
the Dar-Es-Salaam railway (100,000 tons per annum) and the Lobito route for
the remaining export. The traffic forecast and the assumed rates are based
on the findings of a Bank appraisal mission which visited Zaire in April -
May 1974. The following 1974 rates were used for transport projections within
Zaire. It is assumed that the production from the expansion project will
benefit from the marginal rate of g 44.5/ton through Matadi.

7.        Fixed Assets - See Annex 5-1 for the expansion project.
8.        Working Capital Requirements - See Annex 5-2.
9.        Tax Regulation - As provided in the Zairian Investment Code, a
government decree dated June 1969, set out special 4 year tax exemptions
for GECAMINES to enable it to implement its 1970-1974 expansion program. The
Company was exempted for the 1970-74 period from all import duties and turn
over taxes on the imported capital goods and services to be used to increase
annual copper and cobalt production from 360,000 tons and 11,000 tons to
460,000 tons and 16,000 tons respectively. Furthermore, the Company was
exempted from the following taxes and duties to be levied on the incremental
copper and cobalt productions over 360,000 tons and 11,000 tons respectively:
a)   export duty of 40% for copper and 30% for cobalt on a "base
value" equivalent to (FOB value/1.41) for copper and (FOB
value/1.31) for cobalt;
b)   registration fee of 1% on the base value of mineral sales;
c)   turn-over tax (Contribution sur Chiffre d'Affaire-CCA) of 7%
for copper and cobalt levied on the net sales revenues;
d)   corporate profits tax (Contribution Professionnelle) of 40% of
profits before taxes, and



ANNEX 6-1
Page 4
e)   supplementary export tax (Droit de Sortie Complementaire - DSC)
indexed on a base value of 9 340/ton for copper equivalent to a
LEM price for copper of 9 500/ton or USi 45.3/lb; below
9 340/ton no DSC is paid. The method of calculating the supple-
mentary export tax is given in Annex 3-1, pages 13 - 16.
10.       According to the provisions of the investment code the Government
granted GECAMINES the following tax exemptions for the 1975-1982 period.
a)   export duties, turn over tax and profit tax on copper, cobalt
and their alloys in excess of an annual production of 460,000
tons of copper and 16,000 tons of cobalt
b)   import duties and turn over tax on imported capital goods
required for the expansion.
GECAMINES cannot use freely the savings accruing from the above tax exemptions,
but has to allocate it for either debt service payments related to the project,
or the financing of replacement or project investments.
11.       Following the continuing production cost increases, during the past
3 years GECAMINES has been discussing with the Government a modification of the
supplementary export tax. This tax had been originally introduced to distribute
immediately the benefits of higher than average copper prices between GECAMINES
and the Government. Agreement, in principle, has been reached to adopt one of
the two modifications as of January 1, 1975.
a)   increase of the base value of the supplementary export tax to
65,000 FB/ton of copper, or
b)   the substitution of the supplementary export by a bi-monthly
dividend according to the Company's operating results.
For the purpose of the financial projections the first alternative has been
chosen.
12.       Depreciation.  GECAMINES uses linear depreciation for all fixed
assets according to its technical life. These depreciation rates are sub-
stantially lower than the legally allowed rates. The following table gives
examples of GECAMINES depreciation practices:

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
EXPANSION PROJECT - FINANCIAL RATE OF RETURN
AND SENSITIVITY TESTS
Input Data
1.        The incremental, internal financial rate of return calculations
for the project are based on a financial cost/benefit stream in 1974 real
terms. This stream has been obtained by:
(a)  eliminating the general inflationary cmponent from the operating cost
streams (Annex 6-1, para 4) and calculating revenues based on copper and
cobalt prices in real terms (Annexes 3-2 and 3-3); and
(b) taking into account capital cost of 915.6 million which GECAMINES would
have to invest in the existing operations to maintain the present production
level if the expansion project were not implemented;
(c) assuming a scrap value of X35 million after 15 years, i.e., by end 1988
and based on GECAMINES' applicable depreciation.
All calculations are based on a 15 year period including 3-3/4 years for
construction.

ANNEX 6-6

ZAIRE: GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
Effects of Possible Short-Term Revenue Decline on GECAMINES Ability to
Help Finance the Project from Internal Cash Generation
As a member of CIPEC, Zaire has announced its intention of reducing
its shipments of copper by 10% effective December 1, 1974. While this measure
so far is in effect only through May 1975 (i.e. for six months) in view of the
present outlook of world economic conditions, it cannot be excluded that the
10% decrease in sales will be maintained for the whole of 1975. To evaluate
the effect of this possibility on GECAMINES, revisions were made in the pro-
jected income statements for 1975 and 1976 based on two different sets of
assumptions (Case I and II below):
Case I
GECAMINES sales are assumed to be reduced by 10% in 1975, while
maintaining production and the price of copper as assumed in the financial
projections,i.e. 424,800 tons of copper at a sales price of US$0.73 per pound.
The change in net income is US$2.1 million (i.e. a reduction from
US$10.4 million to US$8.3 million). Thus, prices would have to increase by
only about 1.0% at the reduced sales rate to restore GECAMINES' profits to
their formerly assumed level.
However, the company's cash flow would change more materially in
that the 47,000 tons of copper, which are produced but not sold, must be
financed and added to inventory. As a result, quick assets would be reduced
by US$44.2 million. Nevertheless, since GECAMINES had cash and accounts re-
ceivable of US$385 million in the end of 1974, which was about US$130 million
in excess of its operational needs, GECAMINES ability to provide its portion
of the project financing would not be seriously affected by a sales cut-back
of this magnitude. This 130 million dollars cash would comprise over 60% of
GECAMINES' remaining cash contribution of 216 million dollars (US$240 million
less the amount GECAMINES invested in project facilities during 1974, US$24
million).
Case II
In addition to a 10% cut-back in sales for 1975, an average price
of $0.68 per pount in 1975 and $0.71 per pound in 1976 are assumed. These
are $0.05 per pound, or 7% less than the prices used in the projections. It
is also assumed that production returns to normal in 1976. The result is
shcon in Table II. This probably represents the worst possible case for
GECAMINES.



ANNEX 6-6
Page 2
The change in net income over the two-year period is US$45.4 million
(i.e. from US$26.4 million to minus US$19 million). This combined with the
US$44.2 million necessary to finance the 10% of copper production unsold in
1975, would cause a reduction in the company's quick assets of US$89.6 million.
Nevertheless, GECAMINES has already financed during 1974 some US$24 million
in facilities for the expansion and had, at the end of the year, some US$130
million in cash in excess of its operational requirements. Thus, only some
US$86 million in additional cash generation is needed over the 1975-7 period
which should be available.
As a result, GECAMINES will still be able to meet its financing
needs for the project even under these adverse assumptions. If required,
however, GECAMINES could also resort to short-term debt to cover any possible
short fall during this period.

ANNEX 8-3
Page 1
ZAIRE:  GECAMINES EXPANSION PROJECT
ECONOMIC RATE OF RETURN AND
SENSITIVITY ANALYSIS
A. ASSUMPTIONS
1.        The economic cost/benefit stream has been derived from the finan-
cial projections and cost/benefit stream (Annexes 6-2, 6-4). All costs and
revenues are expressed in 1974 real terms to eliminate general inflationary
cost increases.  All calculations are based on a 15 year period including
3-3/4 years for construction.
2.        The following detailed assumptions have been made:
a) the base case has been calculated assuming no shadow pricing
of foreign exchange. However, to demonstrate the sensitivity of
the project to exchange rate changes, Chapter B gives also the
incremental economic rate of return assuming a shadow price rate
of 1.3.
b) Revenues. The financial and economic benefit streams are
equivalent, since GECAMINES will export the entire output of the
expansion project and all sales are valued at the world market
price (LME for copper, Belgian cobalt quotation).
c) Investment Cost. The project cost given in Annex 5-1 has
been reduced by price escalations totalling Z 33.9  million and
the 3% statistical tax on the fob value on imported goods esti-
mated at Z 1.83 million. GECAMINES will pay no other import
duties and taxes for the expansion project. A scrap value of
Z 35 million by end 1988 has been assumed and the alternative
investment cost of g 15.6 million has been taken into account
in the cost/benefit stream of the present operations (without
the project).
d) Personnel Cost. No shadow pricing of labor has been assumed
since the project requires primarily skilled local labor which
has to be trained by GECAMINES.
e) Indirect taxes estimated at about s 4.2 million per year
have been eliminated from the operating cost stream.
f) Other Operating Cost Items. Cost components such as fuel,
electricity and transport cost are not subsidized and conse-
quently no adjustments have been made to obtain their economic
Cost. Since GECAMINES imports nearly 90% of material, supplies
and spare parts excluding electricity, it can be assumed that
they are valued at world market prices.



ANNEX 8-3
Page 2
3.       The project includes major social investments and operating expenses
for communal services. These components constitute a benefit to the country
and should (i) either be taken into account in the economic benefit stream,
or at least (ii) be pliminated from the cost streams. For the purpose of
this report the capital and operating cost for social services related to
the project have been included for the basic economic return calculation,
since in the case of an enclave type project the direct benefits to the Company
may outweigh the indirect benefits to the Zairian economy. Nevertheless, as
part of the sensitivity analysis an economic rate of return has been calculated
(see Case 3) excluding the investment cost for social services (estimated at
3 35 million in real terms) and the operating cost for communal services
(estimated at 23% of labor cost).
